Nikah — Marriage

Dispute over Silence vs. Refusal in Marriage Consent
Ruling on burden of proof in marriage consent dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on whose word is accepted when spouses dispute over consent by silence, identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the requirement of an oath, and apply the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
In disputes over consent by silence, the woman's word is taken with her oath – the Sahibayn's view is the mufta bihi.

This lesson explores a critical procedural rule in Hanafi marriage law: when a husband and wife disagree about whether the wife's silence at the time of the marriage contract indicated consent or refusal. The Hanafi school prioritizes the wife's denial of the contract as the original state, but a subtle internal disagreement exists over whether she must swear an oath to support her claim. Understanding this dispute clarifies how burden of proof operates in family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute
Recognize that the spouses are disagreeing about the legal effect of the wife's silence: did she consent (silence = acceptance) or did she reject (silence = not acceptance)? The husband claims the marriage is binding; the wife claims it is not.
The dispute arises because silence in Hanafi law is interpreted as consent only for a virgin, but here the wife claims she did not consent at all.
2
State the Hanafi Position
The primary Hanafi position is that the wife's word is taken. This is because she denies the occurrence of a binding contract (an incident), and she is adhering to the original state of being unmarried.
The burden of proof falls on the one claiming the incident (husband), not the one denying it.
3
Analyze the Proof (Talil)
The husband claims an incident (luzum al-'aqd and ownership of her private part), which is a new matter. The wife denies it. The original state (al-asl) is the absence of such a contract. Since she is the one claiming the original, her word is preferred.
This is a standard usul principle: al-asl baqa' ma kana 'ala ma kana.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa did not require the wife to take an oath for her claim; her mere denial suffices. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) required her to swear an oath. The fatwa is upon their view (with oath). Zufar held the opposite: the husband's word is taken.
The dispute among Hanafi imams is not over whose word is taken (the woman), but whether an oath is needed.
5
Determine the Fatwa Hierarchy
The text explicitly states 'wa al-fatwa 'ala qawlihima' – the fatwa is upon the saying of the Sahibayn. Thus the operative ruling is that the woman's word is accepted with her oath.
Even though Abu Hanifa's view is from a higher authority (founder of the school), the later scholars adopted the Sahibayn's position for practical reasons, perhaps to prevent frivolous claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول لها إن اختلفا في السكوت، أي للمرأة بيمينها على المفتى به (ط) وقال زفر: القول قوله؛ لأن السكوت أصل والرد شاهداً له، ولنا أنه يدعي عليها لزوم العقد وملك بضعها وهو أمر حادث، وهي تنكر حدوثه، فكانت هي المستمسكة بالأصل فيكون القول قولها، ثم أيهما أقام البينة قبلت بينته، وإن أقاماها معاً فالبينة بينة المرأة، وإن لم يكن لهما بينة فالقول قولها بلا يمين عند أبي حنيفة وقالا عليها اليمين والفتوى على قولهما. [ البحر الرائق: ١٦٩/٣ ، رمز الحقائق: ٢٠١/١]
And the saying is hers if they differ concerning silence, i.e., for the woman with her oath according to the fatwa. Zufar said: The saying is his because silence is the original state and rejection is a witness for him. For us, he claims against her the binding of the contract and ownership of her private part, which is an incident matter, and she denies its occurrence, so she is the one adhering to the original, thus the saying is hers. Then whichever of them establishes evidence, his evidence is accepted. If both establish evidence together, then the evidence is the woman's evidence. If there is no evidence for either, then the saying is hers without an oath according to Abu Hanifa, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Upon her is an oath, and the fatwa is upon their saying.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute between husband and wife regarding the wife's consent to the marriage contract when she remained silent. The ruling is that the woman's word is taken, with her oath, as the relied-upon position. Zufar disagreed, arguing that silence is the original state and rejection is a claim against it. The Hanafi response is that the husband claims a binding contract and ownership of her private part, which is an incident (hadith) that she denies, so she is the one upholding the original (absence of the incident). Therefore, her word prevails. If either brings proof, it is accepted; if both bring proof, the woman's proof is preferred. If neither has proof, the woman's word is taken without oath according to Abu Hanifa, but the two Sahibayn require her oath, and the fatwa follows their view. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position on burden of proof in such disputes.

Key Terms
السكوت (al-sukūt): Silence of the woman when informed of the marriage contract, which is taken as consent if she is a virgin.
يمين (yamīn): Oath; the woman must swear by Allah that she did not consent.
أصل (aṣl): Original state or presumption; here, the absence of the marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fatwa in this dispute, what is required from the woman when she claims she rejected the marriage?
A) She must bring two witnesses.
B) Her word is accepted without any condition.
C) Her word is accepted with her oath.
D) The husband's word is taken with his oath.

Key Takeaway
In a dispute over consent by silence, the woman's word is taken because she denies the incident of the contract, and she must swear an oath per the mufta bihi opinion of the Sahibayn.

Nikah — Marriage

Guardian’s Authority to Marry Minors: A Three-Way Madhhab Dispute
Ruling on the guardian's right to contract marriage for minor children.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on which guardians may marry minors compulsorily, explain the Maliki and Shafi'i counter-arguments, and evaluate the Hanafi response including the role of the option of annulment.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school extends compulsory marriage authority to any 'asabah guardian, but grants the minor an option to annul upon majority.

Who has the right to contract a marriage for a minor child without their consent? The three major Sunni schools differ significantly. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi view — that any agnatic guardian (from father to distant cousin) can do so — against the Maliki restriction to only the father and the Shafi'i middle ground. The key to the Hanafi position lies not just in textual evidence, but in the safeguard of the 'option of annulment' (khiyar al-faskh) that protects the minor from potential harm.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the ruling: any 'asabah guardian may marry a minor boy or girl compulsorily. Note that this is 'jawaz' (permissible) for the guardian, not obligatory.
The term 'jabran' (compulsorily) means without the child's consent, due to their incapacity.
2
Identify the Opponent Schools and Their Views
Malik restricts to the father alone for the minor girl only; Shafi'i allows father and grandfather for both minors. Understand their reasoning: for Malik, need-based; for Shafi'i, compassion and property analogy.
Malik cites the hadith 'al-bikr yuzawwijuhā abūhā'. Shafi'i argues that those who cannot manage property cannot manage marriage.
3
Follow the Hanafi Proof
The Hanafi school uses the hadith 'al-Inkāh ilā al-'aṣabāt' (marriage is for the 'asabat) and analogizes all 'asabah to the father. They also cite the practice of Companions like Umar and Ali.
The hadith is general (amm) and includes all 'asabah in the Hanafi usul.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response to Opponents
To Malik: the grandfather is analogous to father; minor girls also need marriage. To Shafi'i: the crucial distinction is that marriage is a single act, so defect can be remedied by the option of annulment after majority, unlike property which circulates.
The option of annulment (khiyar al-faskh) is the Hanafi linchpin: it allows the minor to ratify or cancel the marriage upon puberty.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi, more guardians can marry minors; the minor has the right to annul if married by a non-father/grandfather guardian. Under Maliki, only father can marry a girl, and the girl has no option of annulment from the father's marriage.
Abu Yusuf disagreed even within Hanafi, denying the option entirely, but the mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللولي إلخ [ أي يجوز للولي عدلا كان أو فاسقا أباً كان أو غيره من الأولياء. (ط)] أطلق الولي إلخ: ليتناول الأب والجد وغيرهما من العصبات أي يجوز للولي خاصة أن ينكح الصغير والصغيرة جبراً، وهذا عندنا؛ لأن ولاية الإجبار عليهما لصغرهما، وقال مالك: لا يجوز لغير الأب؛ لأن ولاية الإجبار عنده مخصوصة بالأب وحده على الصغيرة وحدها دون الصغير، ويقول: إن الولاية على الحرة إنما ثبتت باعتبار الحاجة ولا حاجة لصغيرة؛ لانعدام الشهوة إلا أن ولاية الأب ثبتت نصاً وهو قوله عليها "البكر يزوجها أبوها"، أن أبا بكر له زوج عائشة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، والجد ليس في معناه، فلا يلحق به، قلنا: بل هو موافق القياس؛ لأن النكاح ينتظم المصالح، ولا يتفق الكفؤ في كل زمان فأثبتنا الولاية له في حالة الصغر إحرازاً للكفؤ. وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز تزويجها لغير الأب والجد؛ لأن النظر لا يتم بالتفويض إلى غيرهما؛ لقصور الشفقة لبعد قرابة غيرهما، ألا ترى أن غيره هما لا يملك التصرف في مال الصغير مع أن المال أدنى رتبة فكيف يملك التصرف في النفس مع كونه أعلى رتبة ولنا أن القرابة داعية إلى النظر كما في الأب والجد وما فيه من القصور أظهرناه بأن للصغير والصغيرة خيار الفسخ بخلاف التصرف في المال لأنه يكرر بتداول الأيدي، فلا يمكن تدارك الخلل بعد تداول الأيدي، فلا يثبت ولاية التصرف لغير الأب والجد في مال الصغير، وأما النكاح فيقع مرة ولا يتكرر، فلو وقع فيه خلل يمكن تداركه بالإعراض بعد البلوغ، فيثبت الولاية فيه لغير الأب والجد، ومذهبنا منقول عن عمر وعلي والعبادلة وأبي هريرة رضي الله عنهم وكفا بهم حجة وقدوة، وأيضاً أجمع العلماء على العمل بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: "الإنكاح إلى العصبات في حق الكبيرة"، فكذا في حق الصغيرة؛ لأنها أعجز وأمس حاجة. (عيني)
And for the guardian... [i.e., it is permissible for the guardian, whether just or sinful, father or otherwise from the guardians. (T)] He generalized 'the guardian' to include the father, grandfather, and others from the 'asabat, meaning it is permissible for the guardian specifically to contract marriage for the minor boy and minor girl compulsorily. This is according to us because the authority of compulsion over them is due to their minority. Malik said: It is not permissible for other than the father because the authority of compulsion according to him is specific to the father alone over the minor girl alone, not the minor boy. He says: The authority over a free woman is established by consideration of need, and there is no need for a minor girl due to the absence of desire, except that the father's authority is established by text, namely His saying (peace be upon him): 'The virgin is married by her father,' and that Abu Bakr married Aisha to the Prophet (peace be upon him), and the grandfather is not in his meaning, so he is not joined to him. We said: Rather, it is in accordance with analogy because marriage encompasses benefits, and a suitable match is not available in every time. So we established authority for him in the state of minority to secure the suitable match. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible to marry her to other than the father and grandfather because consideration is not complete by delegating to others due to the deficiency of compassion because of the distance of relationship of others. Do you not see that others besides these two do not possess the authority to dispose of the minor's property, even though property is of a lower rank, so how can they possess authority over the person while it is of a higher rank? For us: Relationship prompts consideration as in the father and grandfather, and what deficiency exists we have manifested it by the fact that the minor boy and minor girl have the option of annulment (khiyar al-faskh), contrary to disposal of property because it is repeated by circulation among hands, so a defect cannot be remedied after circulation, so authority to dispose of a minor's property is not established for other than father and grandfather. As for marriage, it occurs once and does not repeat, so if a defect occurs in it, it can be remedied by disclaiming after majority, so authority is established in it for other than father and grandfather. Our madhhab is transmitted from Umar, Ali, the 'Abadilah (Abdullah ibn Umar, ibn Abbas, etc.), and Abu Hurayrah (may Allah be pleased with them) and sufficient they are as proof and example. Also, the scholars have agreed upon acting upon his saying (peace be upon him): 'Marriage is for the 'asabat with respect to the major woman,' so likewise with respect to the minor woman because she is more incapable and more in need.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi school holds that any guardian from the 'asabat (agnatic relatives) may marry minor children compulsorily (jabran), meaning they can contract the marriage without the child's consent due to their minority. This includes the father, grandfather, and others. Imam Malik restricts this to the father alone, and only for the minor girl, not the boy. He argues that authority over a free woman is based on need, and a minor has no need, but the father's authority is by text (hadith). He also rejects the grandfather by analogy. The Hanafi response is that the grandfather is included by analogy because marriage serves interests and finding a suitable match is not always possible later; so authority is granted to secure it. Imam al-Shafi'i restricts to father and grandfather only, arguing that those with weaker compassion cannot be trusted with such a high matter (marriage) when they cannot even dispose of property. The Hanafi response is that the crucial difference is the option of annulment (khiyar al-faskh) upon majority for minors married by non-father/grandfather guardians. This option remedies any defect, which is not possible in property transactions (since property circulates). Thus, the Hanafi position is supported by the hadith 'Marriage is for the 'asabat' and by the practice of the Companions.

Key Terms
ولاية الإجبار (wilāyat al-ijbār): The authority of the guardian to contract marriage for a minor without their consent, due to minority.
خيار الفسخ (khiyār al-faskh): The option to annul a marriage contract, granted to a minor upon reaching puberty if the guardian was other than father or grandfather.
العصبات (al-‘aṣabāt): Agnatic relatives who inherit by virtue of being male and related through males (e.g., father, grandfather, brother, uncle, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following guardians may NOT marry a minor girl compulsorily?
A) Her paternal uncle
B) Her maternal uncle
C) Her grandfather
D) Her brother

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits any 'asabah guardian to marry minor children compulsorily, but grants the minor the option to annul the marriage after puberty unless the guardian was the father or grandfather, a position that balances authority with protection.

Nikah — Marriage
beginner
6 min
Order of Guardians in Marriage
Guardian priority list with inheritance analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi order of marriage guardians and identify the principle that it follows the 'asabah inheritance sequence.

Core Idea
Marriage guardianship follows the asabah order of inheritance.

The Hanafi school ranks marriage guardians (awliya') in a specific order, modeled after the inheritance hierarchy of 'asabah. This lesson presents that order and its basis in the hadith: 'Marriage (right) belongs to the asabah.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the first tier of guardians
The text states the order begins with the son, then son's son however low. Note that this applies to insane or idiotic minors, not normal minors.
The son excludes the father by reduction (hajb nuqsan) in inheritance, hence the priority.
2
List the subsequent tiers
After the son and his descendants come the father, grandfather, then full/consanguine brothers (excluding maternal), then paternal uncles (excluding maternal), then uncles of father and grandfather, then the master of emancipation and his 'asabah, and finally distant relatives (dhul arham).
Maternal relatives are not included in the asabah order.
3
Examine the dalil
The basis is the hadith from Ali (RA): 'al-Inkah ila al-'asabat' – marriage (guardianship) belongs to the 'asabah. This is a dalil naqli that establishes the hierarchy.
The hadith is both mauquf and marfu'.
4
Apply to a scenario
Given an insane minor girl, her son (if any) is the first guardian, then her father, then her brother, etc. If no asabah, then the master or dhul arham.
This order is only for insane/idiotic minors; normal minors are under father/grandfather.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بترتيب الإرث [ الحجب فيقدم ابن المجنونة على أبيها؛ لأنه يحجبه حجب نقصان. (ط)] يعني أولهم الابن وابن الابن وإن سفل، ولا يتصور هذا إلا في المعتوه والمعتوهة أو المجنون والمجنونة لا في الصغار، ثم الأب وأبو الأب وإن علا، ثم الإخوة إلا الأخ من أم ثم الأعمام إلا العم من أم ثم أعمام الأب، ثم أعمام الجد، ثم مولى ولي العتاقة يستوي فيه الذكر والأنثى، ثم عصبة المولى، ثم ذو الأرحام على ما سيجيء، والأصل فيه ما روي عن علي رضي الله عنه موقوفاً ومرفوعاً: "الإنكاح إلى العصبات". [رمز الحقائق: ۲۰۲/۱] في غير الأب والجد : [ أي للصغير والصغيرة خيار فسخ النكاح بعد البلوغ إذا علما بالنكاح قبل البلوغ أو بعده إذا زوجهما غير الأب والجد، وهذا عند حنيفة ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف لا خيار لهما في الكل؛ لأن النكاح عقد لازم، وقد صدر من الولي فلا يفسخ، ولهما: أن الولاية نظرية وقرابة غيرهما ناقصة والنقصان يشعر بقصور الشفقة، فعسى أن يتطرق الخلل إلى مقاصد النكاح بسبب قصور الشفقة فيتدراك بخيار البلوغ، وأما الأب والجد فقرابتهما كامل وشفقتهما وافر فلا يتطرق الخلل في المقاصد فيثبت الخيار بتزويج الأم والقاضي أيضاً في الأصح؛ لنقصان الرأي في الأم وقصور الشفقة في القضاء. (عيني ومستخلص) بشرط القضاء أي لهما خيار الفسخ بشرط أن يحكم القاضي بالفسخ؛ لأن في أصله ضعفا فيتوقف على القضاء وهو الأصح وعليه الفتوى. (ط،ع) إن علمت بكراً: لدلالة السكوت حينئذ على الرضا، وقيد بعلمها؛ لأنها لو لم تعلم بالنكاح لا يكون رضاً؛ لأنها لا تتمكن من التصرف بحكم الخيار إلا بعد العلم به والولي ينفرد بالنكاح فعذرت، وإنما قيد بالبكر؛ لأنها لو كانت ثيباً كما لو دخل بها الزوج قبل البلوغ أو كانت ثيباً وقت العقد لا يبطل خيارها بالسكوت إلا أن ترضى بلسانها، أو يوجد ما يدل على الرضا كالتمكين من الوطء طوعاً أو المطالبة بالمهر أو النفقة. (فتح) ولو دلالة: [ أي ولو كان الرضاء دلالة بأن يجيء منه ما يدل على الرضا كتسليم الصداق والنفقة ونحوهما. (ط،ع)] اعتباراً لهذه الحالة بحالة الابتداء، ثم خيار البلوغ في حق البكر لا يمتد إلى آخر المجلس، ولا يبطل بالقيام في حق الثيب والغلام وينبغي أن تختار نفسها مع رؤية الدم، ثم الفرقة بخيار البلوغ لا تكون طلاقاً؛ لأنه يصح من الأنثى، ولا طلاق إليها رمز الحقائق: ۲۰۲/۱] وتوارثا إلخ [ أي يرث كل منهما من صاحبه إن مات أحدهما (ط)] لانتهاء النكاح بالموت بدليل حمل الوطء قبل الفسخ سواء مات أحدهما قبل البلوغ أو بعده فيجب المهر كله وإن مات قبل الدخول، بخلاف النكاح الموقوف والفاسد فإنه لو مات أحدهما في الموقوف والفاسد لا يرثه الآخر، ولا يرث بعد فسخ النكاح؛ لأن سبب الوراثة قد زال. (فتح)
By order of inheritance [al-ḥajb (exclusion) is applied, so the son of the insane woman is preferred over her father because he excludes him by reduction. (T)] Meaning first of them is the son and son's son however low, and this is only conceivable with the insane or idiot, not with minors. Then the father and grandfather however high, then full/consanguine brothers except maternal brother, then paternal uncles except maternal uncle, then uncles of the father, then uncles of the grandfather, then the master (mawlā) of emancipation (male and female equal), then the 'asabah of the master, then dhū al-arḥām (distant relatives) as will come. The basis of this is what is narrated from Ali (RA) as mauqūf and marfū‘: 'al-Inkāh ilā al-‘aṣabāt' (Marriage is for the ‘aṣabah). [Ramz al-Ḥaqā’iq: 202/1] For other than the father and grandfather: [i.e., for the minor boy and minor girl, there is an option to annul the marriage after majority if they knew of the marriage before majority or after, if married by other than the father and grandfather. This is according to Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: No option for them at all because marriage is a binding contract, issued from the guardian, so it cannot be annulled. For them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): Guardianship is for welfare, and the relationship of others is deficient, and deficiency indicates weakness of compassion, so defects may affect the purposes of marriage due to weak compassion, so it can be remedied by the option at majority. As for father and grandfather, their relationship is complete and compassion abundant, so defects do not affect the purposes, so the option does not apply. The option is also established when the mother or judge marries them, in the more correct view, due to deficiency of opinion in the mother and weakness of compassion in the judge. (Ayni and Mustakhlas) With the condition of judicial decision (qaḍā’), i.e., they have the option to annul on condition that the judge rules the annulment, because its basis has weakness, so it depends on the judge's ruling, and this is the more correct view and upon it is the fatwa. (T, A) If she knew and was a virgin: because silence then indicates consent, and it is restricted by her knowing because if she did not know of the marriage, there is no consent, as she cannot exercise the option except after knowledge, and the guardian acted alone, so she is excused. It is restricted to a virgin because if she was a non-virgin, as if the husband had intercourse before majority or she was non-virgin at the time of contract, her option is not nullified by silence unless she verbally consents, or something indicating consent appears, such as willingly allowing intercourse, or demanding dower or maintenance. (Fath) And even by indication: [i.e., even if the consent is by indication, such as something from him that indicates consent like handing over the dower or maintenance and the like. (T,A)] Considering this state as the initial state. Then the option of majority for a virgin does not extend to the end of the session, and is not nullified by standing, but for a non-virgin and a youth, it is not so, and she should choose herself upon seeing blood. Then separation by option of majority is not a divorce because it is valid from the female, and she does not have the power of divorce. [Ramz al-Ḥaqā’iq: 202/1] And they inherit from each other [i.e., each inherits from the other if one dies (T)] because the marriage ends by death, as evidenced by carrying the intercourse before annulment, whether one dies before majority or after, the full dower is obligatory even if death occurs before intercourse, contrary to suspended and invalid marriage (mawqūf and fāsid), for if one dies in suspended or invalid marriage, the other does not inherit, and does not inherit after annulment of marriage because the cause of inheritance has ceased. (Fath)
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage covers two major topics: the order of guardians (awliyā') and the option of annulment (khiyār al-faskh) for minors upon majority. First, guardians are ranked by order of inheritance: son, then son's son, then father, grandfather, brothers (excluding maternal), paternal uncles (excluding maternal), etc., down to the master of emancipation and distant relatives, based on the hadith 'al-Inkāh ilā al-‘aṣabāt'. This applies to insane or idiotic minors, not to normal minors who cannot be in that order. Second, if a minor is married by a guardian other than the father or grandfather, the minor has the option to annul the marriage upon reaching majority, provided they knew of the marriage. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; Abu Yusuf denied any option. The option requires a judge's ruling (qaḍā') because its basis is weak; this is the muftā bihī position. The option also applies when the mother or judge marries them. A virgin's silence after knowledge indicates consent, nullifying the option, but a non-virgin must explicitly consent or indicate by action (e.g., demanding dower). The option period for a virgin ends with her choosing immediately; for a non-virgin it does not expire by standing. Separation through this option is not a divorce. Lastly, as long as the marriage is valid, both spouses inherit from each other, even before the option is exercised, because the marriage contract continues until annulment. Inheritance does not occur in suspended or invalid marriages, or after annulment.

Key Terms
حجب نقصان (ḥajb nuqṣān): Exclusion by reduction: the son reduces the father's share in inheritance; here used metaphorically for priority in guardianship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, who is the first guardian for an insane minor?
A) Father
B) Grandfather
C) Son (if any)
D) Brother

Key Takeaway
Marriage guardians for insane minors follow the asabah order: son, then son's son, then father, then grandfather, then paternal brothers, etc., excluding maternal relatives.

Khiyār al-Faskh: Option of Annulment upon Majority
Option of annulment ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule of khiyar al-faskh for minors married by non-father/grandfather guardians and state the condition of judicial decree (qada).

Core Idea
A minor married by a guardian other than father or grandfather may annul the marriage upon majority, but only with a judge's ruling.

The Hanafi school grants an option of annulment to minors who were married by guardians lacking full compassion, such as brothers or uncles. This lesson examines the ruling, the intra-madhhab disagreement, and the practical requirements for its exercise, including the crucial condition that annulment requires a judge's decree.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The passage states that a minor (boy or girl) has the option to annul a marriage upon majority if married by someone other than the father or grandfather. This includes the mother and the judge.
This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; Abu Yusuf denies any option.
2
Understand the basis of the option
The reason is that guardians other than father/grandfather have deficient compassion (shafaqah), so the law gives the minor a remedy upon reaching maturity.
Father and grandfather have complete compassion; no option needed.
3
Analyze the condition of qada
The option is not self-executing; it requires a judge's ruling (qada) because the option is weak in its basis. This is the more correct view and the basis for fatwa.
Without a judge's ruling, the marriage remains valid if not annulled.
4
Distinguish between virgin and non-virgin
If a virgin knew of the marriage and remained silent, her silence indicates consent, nullifying the option. For a non-virgin (thayyib), silence is not consent; she must verbally consent or indicate by actions like demanding dower.
The option period for a virgin ends immediately upon knowledge and choice; for a non-virgin it is not extinguished by standing.
5
Apply the inheritance rule
If one spouse dies before annulment, the other inherits because the marriage was valid. This is unlike suspended (mawquf) or invalid (fasid) marriages where no inheritance occurs.
The option does not affect inheritance rights retroactively.

By order of inheritance [al-ḥajb (exclusion) is applied, so the son of the insane woman is preferred over her father because he excludes him by reduction. (T)] Meaning first of them is the son and son's son however low, and this is only conceivable with the insane or idiot, not with minors. Then the father and grandfather however high, then full/consanguine brothers except maternal brother, then paternal uncles except maternal uncle, then uncles of the father, then uncles of the grandfather, then the master (mawlā) of emancipation (male and female equal), then the 'asabah of the master, then dhū al-arḥām (distant relatives) as will come. The basis of this is what is narrated from Ali (RA) as mauqūf and marfū‘: 'al-Inkāh ilā al-‘aṣabāt' (Marriage is for the ‘aṣabah). [Ramz al-Ḥaqā’iq: 202/1] For other than the father and grandfather: [i.e., for the minor boy and minor girl, there is an option to annul the marriage after majority if they knew of the marriage before majority or after, if married by other than the father and grandfather. This is according to Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: No option for them at all because marriage is a binding contract, issued from the guardian, so it cannot be annulled. For them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): Guardianship is for welfare, and the relationship of others is deficient, and deficiency indicates weakness of compassion, so defects may affect the purposes of marriage due to weak compassion, so it can be remedied by the option at majority. As for father and grandfather, their relationship is complete and compassion abundant, so defects do not affect the purposes, so the option does not apply. The option is also established when the mother or judge marries them, in the more correct view, due to deficiency of opinion in the mother and weakness of compassion in the judge. (Ayni and Mustakhlas) With the condition of judicial decision (qaḍā’), i.e., they have the option to annul on condition that the judge rules the annulment, because its basis has weakness, so it depends on the judge's ruling, and this is the more correct view and upon it is the fatwa. (T, A) If she knew and was a virgin: because silence then indicates consent, and it is restricted by her knowing because if she did not know of the marriage, there is no consent, as she cannot exercise the option except after knowledge, and the guardian acted alone, so she is excused. It is restricted to a virgin because if she was a non-virgin, as if the husband had intercourse before majority or she was non-virgin at the time of contract, her option is not nullified by silence unless she verbally consents, or something indicating consent appears, such as willingly allowing intercourse, or demanding dower or maintenance. (Fath) And even by indication: [i.e., even if the consent is by indication, such as something from him that indicates consent like handing over the dower or maintenance and the like. (T,A)] Considering this state as the initial state. Then the option of majority for a virgin does not extend to the end of the session, and is not nullified by standing, but for a non-virgin and a youth, it is not so, and she should choose herself upon seeing blood. Then separation by option of majority is not a divorce because it is valid from the female, and she does not have the power of divorce. [Ramz al-Ḥaqā’iq: 202/1] And they inherit from each other [i.e., each inherits from the other if one dies (T)] because the marriage ends by death, as evidenced by carrying the intercourse before annulment, whether one dies before majority or after, the full dower is obligatory even if death occurs before intercourse, contrary to suspended and invalid marriage (mawqūf and fāsid), for if one dies in suspended or invalid marriage, the other does not inherit, and does not inherit after annulment of marriage because the cause of inheritance has ceased. (Fath)

Hierarchy of Marriage Guardians and Disqualifications
Listing of guardians in order and conditions for disqualification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the order of marriage guardians in the Hanafi school and identify which individuals are disqualified from acting as guardians.

Core Idea
Guardianship in marriage follows a fixed hierarchy from asabah to dhawi al-arham; slaves, minors, insane, and non-Muslims cannot be guardians.

In Hanafi fiqh, the right to give a woman in marriage is reserved for specific relatives in a defined order. This lesson outlines that hierarchy from the father to the most distant uterine relatives, and also explains who is disqualified from being a guardian. Understanding this order is essential for determining the validity of a marriage contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary guardians (asabah)
List the asabah guardians in order: father, paternal grandfather, brother (full and half by father), paternal uncle (full and half by father), and so on. Also includes the emancipating master and his asabah.
The hadith 'Marriage is entrusted to the asabah' is the basis.
2
Determine when non-asabah become guardians
When no asabah exists, guardianship passes to non-asabah relatives: mother, full sister, paternal sister, uterine sibling, then dhawi al-arham (maternal aunts, etc.).
This is the mufta bihi position of the school according to Abu Hanifa.
3
Recognize absolute disqualifications
A slave, a minor, an insane person, and a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman are disqualified from acting as guardians. Understand the rationale for each.
Non-Muslims can be guardians over their own minor Muslim children, but not over adult Muslims.
4
Apply the hierarchy to a scenario
Given a woman with no asabah, trace the guardianship: first her mother, then her full sister, then paternal sister, then uterine siblings, then dhawi al-arham, and finally the judge.
The judge (sultan) is the ultimate guardian when no relative qualifies.

وكافر على مسلمة: الأنه لا نظر في رأيهم، ولهذا لا تقبل شهادتهم. (ط، ع)] وولد مسلم قيد بالمسلمة؛ لأن الكافر له ولاية على ابنته الصغيرة، وكما انتفى ولاية الكافر على المسلمة انتفى ولاية المسلم على الكافر أعني ولاية الإنكاح والتصرف في المال، إلا أن يكون المسلم سيد أمة كافرة أو سلطاناً، ووجه نفي ولاية كافر على مسلمة، قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَنْ يَجْعَلَ اللَّهُ لِلْكَافِرِينَ عَلَى الْمُؤْمِنِينَ سَبِيلاً )) (النساء:١٤١) ولأنه لا نظر في رأيهم. فرع للقاضي تزويج اليتيمة الكافرة حيث لا ولي لها وكان مأذوناً عن السلطان الصغار مطلقاً. بتزويج لم تكن عصبة كان هنا تامة أي إن لم توجد عصبة لا قريبة ولا بعيدة ولا نسبية ولا سببية. (ط،ع) ثم لأب : أي الأخت لأب على المفتى به، وفي "الحضانة": الأخت لأم أولى منها. (ط، ع) لولد الأم: أي الأخت والأخ لأم ذكورهم وإناثهم سواء ثم لأولادهم. (عيني، مستخلص)
السلطان تزويج
Then the brothers except the uterine brother, then paternal uncles except the maternal uncle, then father's paternal uncles, then grandfather's paternal uncles, then the master who grants emancipation, male and female are equal, then the master's asabah, then the dhawi al-arham as will come. The basis of this is what is narrated from Ali, as a mauquf and marfu' hadith: 'Marriage is entrusted to the asabah.' [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 202/1]. There is no guardianship for a slave, a minor, an insane person, or a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman. And if there is no asabah, then guardianship (i.e., in marriage) is for the mother, then for the sister by father and mother, then for the sister by father, then for the child of the mother, then for the dhawi al-arham. ... There is no guardianship for a slave: because they have no guardianship over themselves, so they should not have guardianship over others, as guardianship over others is subsidiary to guardianship over oneself. However, negation of guardianship over the slave is not absolute; rather it is in marriage, because the negation of guardianship over a slave is prevented for hudud, qisas, his aman if he is a ma'dhun, his narration of hadith, and because he has guardianship over his free wife in marital matters like preventing her from going out, being able to have intercourse, and seeking adornment. (Fath) A minor and an insane person: i.e., they have no guardianship over anyone. (T) A non-Muslim over a Muslim woman: because there is no consideration in their opinion, hence their testimony is not accepted. (T, A) And the child of a Muslim is restricted to a Muslim woman; because a non-Muslim has guardianship over his minor daughter. Just as the guardianship of a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman is negated, so is the guardianship of a Muslim over a non-Muslim, i.e., guardianship of marriage and disposition of property, except if the Muslim is the master of a non-Muslim slave woman or a sultan. The basis for negating guardianship of a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman is Allah's statement: 'And Allah will never grant the disbelievers a way over the believers' (4:141), and because there is no consideration in their opinion. A branch: The judge may marry the orphan non-Muslim woman if she has no guardian, and he is authorized by the sultan for minors absolutely. By marriage, if there is no asabah, it is complete here, i.e., if there is no asabah, neither near nor far, neither by lineage nor by cause. (T, A) Then for the father: i.e., the sister by father, according to the mufta bihi. In 'al-Hadana', the sister by mother is more entitled than her. (T, A) For the child of the mother: i.e., the uterine brother and sister, males and females are equal, then their children. (T, A) Then for dhawi al-arham: [i.e., paternal aunts, then maternal uncles, then maternal aunts, then daughters of paternal uncles, then their children in this order, then the master by muwalah. (T)] Know that non-asabah relatives have guardianship in marriage according to Abu Hanifa. This is istihsan. The analogy is that non-asabah have no guardianship, which is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), due to the hadith of Ali: 'Marriage is entrusted to the asabah', and because guardianship is established to protect the family from being related to a non-compatible person, and such protection is only for the asabah. Abu Hanifa argues that this guardianship is a guardianship of consideration, and consideration is realized by entrusting it to one who is specifically related by kinship that evokes affection. Then the author arranged the people of obligatory shares in the book according to the order of asabah, and gave precedence to the mother because she is never excluded (by hijab), then the sister by father and mother (she is excluded in the presence of a son), and so on until he placed dhawi al-arham last because of the disagreement among the Companions about their inheritance. His statement: 'Then to the ruler' is based on the Prophet's saying: 'The sultan is the guardian of those who have no guardian.' It is said that this rule refers to the judge who supervises orphans who have no guardians. Muhammad said: 'There is no guardianship for non-asabah. When there is no asabah, guardianship is for the imam or his deputy, i.e., the judge.' And this is the view of the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i). The executor (wasi) may not marry the orphans unless the testator delegated that to him. (Ayni, Mustakhlas) The sultan's marriage.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage covers the hierarchy of guardians in marriage and the conditions for guardianship. It begins by listing the order of guardians based on asabah (male agnates) from the nearest to the most distant: full brothers, then half-brothers by father, then paternal uncles, then father's paternal uncles, grandfather's paternal uncles, then the master (emancipator), then the asabah of the master, and finally dhawi al-arham (uterine relatives). The hadith 'Marriage is entrusted to the asabah' is cited as the basis for this order. Then the passage states who cannot be guardians: a slave, a minor, an insane person, and a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman. The commentary explains that a slave has no guardianship over his own marriage, so he cannot be a guardian for others, though he has certain marital rights over his wife. A minor and insane person cannot act as guardians because they lack capacity. A non-Muslim cannot be guardian of a Muslim woman based on the Qur'anic verse (4:141). However, a non-Muslim can be guardian of his own minor Muslim daughter. Conversely, a Muslim cannot be guardian of a non-Muslim except in certain cases. Then the passage addresses the situation when there are no asabah: guardianship transfers to the mother, then to full sisters, then paternal sisters, then uterine siblings, then dhawi al-arham. The commentary introduces an intra-Hanafi dispute: According to Abu Hanifa, non-asabah relatives (like mother, sisters, aunts) do have guardianship in marriage by istihsan (juristic preference). The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say that only asabah have guardianship by qiyas (analogy), citing the hadith of Ali. The passage sides with Abu Hanifa's view, as it is the matn's position (the book orders non-asabah guardians). Finally, if no guardian exists from any of these categories, then the ruler (imam or judge) is the guardian, based on the hadith 'The sultan is the guardian of those who have no guardian.' Muhammad holds that the imam or judge is the guardian in the absence of asabah, which is also the view of the other schools. The passage concludes that an executor (wasi) cannot marry orphans unless specifically authorized by the testator.

Key Terms
عصبة (asabah): Male agnate relatives who are entitled to inherit residuary shares; they are the primary guardians in marriage in default of the father/grandfather.
ذو الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): Uterine relatives who are not asabah, such as maternal uncles, aunts, and their descendants; they are last in the order of guardianship.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that sets aside an analogy in favor of a ruling that better serves the objectives of the law, used here to allow non-asabah guardianship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is disqualified from being a marriage guardian for a Muslim woman?
A) Her father
B) Her adult brother
C) Her maternal uncle
D) Her minor son

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi order of guardians begins with asabah, extends to non-asabah by istihsan, and excludes slaves, minors, insane, and non-Muslims over Muslims.

Nikah — Marriage

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Non-Asabah Guardianship
Debate on non-asabah's right to give in marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on non-asabah guardianship, cite the dalil for each position, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa allows non-asabah guardianship by istihsan; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad restrict it to asabah by qiyas.

While the Hanafi school presents a clear hierarchy of guardians, there is a significant internal disagreement about whether non-asabah relatives can act as guardians at all. This lesson examines the arguments of Abu Hanifa, who uses istihsan to extend guardianship, and the two companions, who adhere to the literal hadith. Understanding this dispute is key to knowing who can marry a woman in the absence of asabah.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position as per the matn
The text explicitly includes non-asabah (mother, sisters, etc.) in the order of guardians, indicating that they have guardianship when no asabah exists.
The author says 'and if there is no asabah, then guardianship is for the mother...' showing acceptance.
2
Identify the opposing view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that only asabah have guardianship. In their absence, guardianship passes to the judge (imam), not to non-asabah relatives.
This aligns with the majority of other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali).
3
Compare the proofs
The opponents cite the hadith 'Marriage is entrusted to the asabah' and argue that guardianship protects family standing, which only asabah can uphold. Abu Hanifa uses istihsan: guardianship is about consideration (nazariyya) and affection, which non-asabah also possess.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala, allowing for interpretation.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, a woman without asabah can be married by her mother or sister. Under the companions' view, she must go to the judge. This affects the ease and autonomy of marriage.
The Hanafi school follows Abu Hanifa's view as mufta bihi.

(ط، ع)] وولد مسلم قيد بالمسلمة؛ لأن الكافر له ولاية على ابنته الصغيرة، وكما انتفى ولاية الكافر على المسلمة انتفى ولاية المسلم على الكافر أعني ولاية الإنكاح والتصرف في المال، إلا أن يكون المسلم سيد أمة كافرة أو سلطاناً، ووجه نفي ولاية كافر على مسلمة، قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَنْ يَجْعَلَ اللَّهُ لِلْكَافِرِينَ عَلَى الْمُؤْمِنِينَ سَبِيلاً )) (النساء:١٤١) ولأنه لا نظر في رأيهم. فرع للقاضي تزويج اليتيمة الكافرة حيث لا ولي لها وكان مأذوناً عن السلطان الصغار مطلقاً. بتزويج لم تكن عصبة كان هنا تامة أي إن لم توجد عصبة لا قريبة ولا بعيدة ولا نسبية ولا سببية. (ط،ع) ثم لأب : أي الأخت لأب على المفتى به، وفي "الحضانة": الأخت لأم أولى منها. (ط، ع) لولد الأم: أي الأخت والأخ لأم ذكورهم وإناثهم سواء ثم لأولادهم. (ط)] اعلم أن لغير العصبات من الأقارب ولاية التزويج عند أبي حنيفة، وهذا استحسان، والقياس أن لا ولاية لغير العصبات، وهو قولهما؛ لقوله علي: "الإنكاح إلى العصبات"؛ ولأن الولاية إنما ثبت صوناً للقرابة عن نسبة غير الكفؤ إليها، والصيانة إلى العصبات لا غير، ولأبي حنيفة أن هذه ولاية نظرية، والنظر يتحقق بالتفويض إلى من هو المختص بالقرابة الباعثة على الشفقة ثم المصنف رتب أصحاب الفروض في الكتاب على ترتيب العصبات، وقدم الأم؛ لأنها لا تحجب بحال، ثم الأخت لأب وأم فإنها تحجب عند وجود الابن، وهكذا إلى أن أخر ذوي الأرحام عن الكل؛ لأن في توريثهم اختلافا بين الصحابة، وقوله: "ثم للحاكم" لقوله عليه: "السلطان ولي من لا ولي له وقيل المراد من هذا الحكم القاضي الذي يكون في منشورة من اليتامى الذين لا أولياء لهم، وقال محمد : ليس لغير العصبة ولاية وإذا عدمت العصبة يكون للإمام أو لنائبه، وهو القاضي، وبه قالت الثلاثة وليس للوصي أن يزوج الأيتام إلا أن يفوض إليه الموصي بذلك. (عيني، مستخلص)
السلطان تزويج
Then the brothers except the uterine brother, then paternal uncles except the maternal uncle, then father's paternal uncles, then grandfather's paternal uncles, then the master who grants emancipation, male and female are equal, then the master's asabah, then the dhawi al-arham as will come. The basis of this is what is narrated from Ali, as a mauquf and marfu' hadith: 'Marriage is entrusted to the asabah.' [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 202/1]. There is no guardianship for a slave, a minor, an insane person, or a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman. And if there is no asabah, then guardianship (i.e., in marriage) is for the mother, then for the sister by father and mother, then for the sister by father, then for the child of the mother, then for the dhawi al-arham. ... There is no guardianship for a slave: because they have no guardianship over themselves, so they should not have guardianship over others, as guardianship over others is subsidiary to guardianship over oneself. However, negation of guardianship over the slave is not absolute; rather it is in marriage, because the negation of guardianship over a slave is prevented for hudud, qisas, his aman if he is a ma'dhun, his narration of hadith, and because he has guardianship over his free wife in marital matters like preventing her from going out, being able to have intercourse, and seeking adornment. (Fath) A minor and an insane person: i.e., they have no guardianship over anyone. (T) A non-Muslim over a Muslim woman: because there is no consideration in their opinion, hence their testimony is not accepted. (T, A) And the child of a Muslim is restricted to a Muslim woman; because a non-Muslim has guardianship over his minor daughter. Just as the guardianship of a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman is negated, so is the guardianship of a Muslim over a non-Muslim, i.e., guardianship of marriage and disposition of property, except if the Muslim is the master of a non-Muslim slave woman or a sultan. The basis for negating guardianship of a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman is Allah's statement: 'And Allah will never grant the disbelievers a way over the believers' (4:141), and because there is no consideration in their opinion. A branch: The judge may marry the orphan non-Muslim woman if she has no guardian, and he is authorized by the sultan for minors absolutely. By marriage, if there is no asabah, it is complete here, i.e., if there is no asabah, neither near nor far, neither by lineage nor by cause. (T, A) Then for the father: i.e., the sister by father, according to the mufta bihi. In 'al-Hadana', the sister by mother is more entitled than her. (T, A) For the child of the mother: i.e., the uterine brother and sister, males and females are equal, then their children. (T, A) Then for dhawi al-arham: [i.e., paternal aunts, then maternal uncles, then maternal aunts, then daughters of paternal uncles, then their children in this order, then the master by muwalah. (T)] Know that non-asabah relatives have guardianship in marriage according to Abu Hanifa. This is istihsan. The analogy is that non-asabah have no guardianship, which is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), due to the hadith of Ali: 'Marriage is entrusted to the asabah', and because guardianship is established to protect the family from being related to a non-compatible person, and such protection is only for the asabah. Abu Hanifa argues that this guardianship is a guardianship of consideration, and consideration is realized by entrusting it to one who is specifically related by kinship that evokes affection. Then the author arranged the people of obligatory shares in the book according to the order of asabah, and gave precedence to the mother because she is never excluded (by hijab), then the sister by father and mother (she is excluded in the presence of a son), and so on until he placed dhawi al-arham last because of the disagreement among the Companions about their inheritance. His statement: 'Then to the ruler' is based on the Prophet's saying: 'The sultan is the guardian of those who have no guardian.' It is said that this rule refers to the judge who supervises orphans who have no guardians. Muhammad said: 'There is no guardianship for non-asabah. When there is no asabah, guardianship is for the imam or his deputy, i.e., the judge.' And this is the view of the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i). The executor (wasi) may not marry the orphans unless the testator delegated that to him. (Ayni, Mustakhlas) The sultan's marriage.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage covers the hierarchy of guardians in marriage and the conditions for guardianship. It begins by listing the order of guardians based on asabah (male agnates) from the nearest to the most distant: full brothers, then half-brothers by father, then paternal uncles, then father's paternal uncles, grandfather's paternal uncles, then the master (emancipator), then the asabah of the master, and finally dhawi al-arham (uterine relatives). The hadith 'Marriage is entrusted to the asabah' is cited as the basis for this order. Then the passage states who cannot be guardians: a slave, a minor, an insane person, and a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman. The commentary explains that a slave has no guardianship over his own marriage, so he cannot be a guardian for others, though he has certain marital rights over his wife. A minor and insane person cannot act as guardians because they lack capacity. A non-Muslim cannot be guardian of a Muslim woman based on the Qur'anic verse (4:141). However, a non-Muslim can be guardian of his own minor Muslim daughter. Conversely, a Muslim cannot be guardian of a non-Muslim except in certain cases. Then the passage addresses the situation when there are no asabah: guardianship transfers to the mother, then to full sisters, then paternal sisters, then uterine siblings, then dhawi al-arham. The commentary introduces an intra-Hanafi dispute: According to Abu Hanifa, non-asabah relatives (like mother, sisters, aunts) do have guardianship in marriage by istihsan (juristic preference). The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say that only asabah have guardianship by qiyas (analogy), citing the hadith of Ali. The passage sides with Abu Hanifa's view, as it is the matn's position (the book orders non-asabah guardians). Finally, if no guardian exists from any of these categories, then the ruler (imam or judge) is the guardian, based on the hadith 'The sultan is the guardian of those who have no guardian.' Muhammad holds that the imam or judge is the guardian in the absence of asabah, which is also the view of the other schools. The passage concludes that an executor (wasi) cannot marry orphans unless specifically authorized by the testator.

Key Terms
عصبة (asabah): Male agnate relatives who are entitled to inherit residuary shares; they are the primary guardians in marriage in default of the father/grandfather.
ذو الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): Uterine relatives who are not asabah, such as maternal uncles, aunts, and their descendants; they are last in the order of guardianship.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that sets aside an analogy in favor of a ruling that better serves the objectives of the law, used here to allow non-asabah guardianship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Abu Hanifa's use of istihsan to allow non-asabah guardianship is the mufta bihi position, facilitating marriage for women without asabah.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Marriage Guardianship: Non-Asaba Guardians and the Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on non-asaba guardianship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa permits non-asaba relatives to be marriage guardians, state the Sahibayn's qiyas objection, and identify the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, marriage guardianship is based on compassion, not just lineage — so the mother can be a guardian if no agnate exists.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents the order of marriage guardians and a key intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn states that if there is no asaba (agnatic relative), guardianship goes to specific non-asaba relatives like the mother, sister, and uterine kin. The commentary explains that this ruling is based on Abu Hanifa's istihsan, which gives weight to the compassion of close relatives. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) objected using qiyas, citing a hadith that restricts guardianship to asaba. Understanding this dispute helps you see how different usul principles (istihsan vs. qiyas) produce different fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn's ruling on non-asaba guardians
Read the matn: 'If there is no asaba, then guardianship is for the mother, then the sister for both parents, then for father only, then for the child of the mother, then for dhawi al-arham.' Recognize that this includes relatives beyond the agnates, like the mother and maternal relatives.
The matn itself reflects Abu Hanifa's view.
2
Understand the Sahibayn's qiyas argument
The commentary states: 'The qiyas is that non-asaba have no guardianship, and that is the view of the two companions due to the saying of Ali: "Marriage is for the asaba."' This is a qiyas based on the preservation of lineage honor, which only asaba care about.
Qiyas jali: analogizing from the purpose of guardianship.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's istihsan reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that 'this guardianship is a guardianship of consideration (wilayah nazariyyah), and consideration is realized by delegating to one who is specifically related due to kinship that inspires compassion.' Istihsan prioritizes the higher objective of welfare over strict analogy.
Istihsan is a departure from qiyas due to a stronger proof or maslahah.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical difference: a woman without asaba can be married by her mother or uterine brother (Hanafi), but without this, she would need the judge (Sahibayn/Shafi'i). The relied-upon (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view, as it is more practical and widely accepted.
This dispute is legitimate ikhtilaf; both positions are valid.

الولي الأقرب
ثم أي عند عدم هؤلاء تكون الولاية للحاكم. (ط) للحاكم: أي السلطان والقاضي المأذون بالنكاح ونائب القاضي كالقاضي إن فوض له ذلك. (ط) وللأبعد التزويج: أي إذا غاب الولي الأقرب غيبة منقطعة فللأبعد ولاية التزويج وقال زفر: لا ولاية له؛ لأن ولاية الأقرب قائمة فلا تبطل بغيبته ولنا أن هذه ولاية نظرية، ولا نظر في التفويض إلى من ينتفع برأيه، ثم في تفسير الغيبة المنقطعة اختلاف فقيل: أن يكون الأقرب في بلدة لا تصل إليها قافلة في السنة إلا مرة واحدة، وقيل: هي أدنى مدة السفر، وهو اختيار المصنف وكثير من المتأخرين وعليه الفتوى، وقيل: أن يفوت الكفؤ الخاطب بانتظاره، وهذا أقرب إلى الفقه وقال الشافعي يزوجها الحاكم، وهذا مبني على أنه إذا غاب الولي انتقلت الولاية إلى الحاكم، لكن عندنا تنتقل إلى الأبعد إجماعاً، وقوله: "ولا يبطل بعوده" أي لا يبطل ما عقد الأبعد في غيبة الأقرب بعد عود الأقرب خلافاً لزفر، حيث قال: يبطل عقد الأبعد، ولنا: أن الشرع جعل الأبعد الأقرب فالقدرة على الأصل بعد حصول المقصود بالخلف لا يفيد كما لو صلى بالتيمم ثم قدر على
خلفاً
عن
الماء لا يبطل ما صلى. (عيني، مستخلص) مسافة القصر : وهي ثلاثة أيام ولياليها وعليه الفتوى. (ط)
الابن لا الأب: هذا عند الشيخين وقال محمد أبوها وليها؛ لأنه أشفق منه، ولهذا تعم ولايته في النفس والمال وليس للابن الولاية في المال فكان الأب أولى ولهما أن الابن مقدم بالعصبة، وهذه الولاية مبنية عليها بالنص ولا يعتبر بزيادة الشفقة كأب الأم مع بعض العصبات، ولا فرق بين الجنون الطارئ والأصلي لوجود العجز، وقال زفر: لا يزوجها أحد في الطارئ، وعن أبي يوسف في رواية: أنهما وليان فأيهما زوج صح، وعند حضورهما يقدم الأب احتراماً له، ولو كان مكان الأب جد مع الابن فعلى الخلاف المذكور. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٠٣/١]
Book of Marriage. Chapter of Guardians and Compatible Matches. There is no guardianship for a slave, a minor, an insane person, and a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman. And if there is no asaba (agnatic relative), then guardianship (in marriage) even for a mukatab (slave under contract) then to the sister for father and mother, then for father, then to the child of the mother, then to dhawi al-arham (uterine relatives). For the mother: For the invalid marriage. There is no guardianship for a slave: because they have no guardianship over themselves, so it is more appropriate that they have no guardianship over others; because guardianship over others is a branch of guardianship over oneself. The negation of guardianship over the slave is not intended absolutely, but the guardianship of marriage; because the negation of guardianship over him concerning penalties, retaliation, his security if permitted, and his transmission of hadith, and because he has guardianship over his free wife in matters of marriage like preventing her from going out, allowing sexual intercourse, and requesting adornment. (Fath) And a minor and insane: meaning they have no guardianship over anyone. (Tali'?) The slave absolutely is prohibited because of the validity of his confession. (Fath) And a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman: because there is no consideration in their opinions, and therefore their testimony is not accepted. (Tali', 'Ayni?) And the child is a Muslim, qualified by 'Muslim woman'; because the non-Muslim has guardianship over his minor daughter. And just as the guardianship of a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman is negated, so too the guardianship of a Muslim over a non-Muslim is negated, meaning the guardianship of marrying and disposition of wealth, unless the Muslim is the master of a non-Muslim slave woman or a sultan. The face of negating the guardianship of a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman is His statement: 'And Allah will never give the non-Muslims a way over the believers' (4:141) and because there is no consideration in their opinions. A sub-issue: The judge may marry the non-Muslim orphan where there is no guardian for her and he is authorized by the sultan to marry the minors absolutely. 'If there was no asaba' here is complete meaning if there is no asaba, neither near nor far, nor by lineage nor by cause. (Tali', 'Ayni) Then for the father: meaning the sister for father is the relied-upon position. In 'al-Hadana', the sister for mother takes precedence over her. (Tali', 'Ayni) For the child of the mother: meaning the brother and sister for mother, males and females are equal, then their children. (Tali', 'Ayni) Then for dhawi al-arham: meaning paternal aunts, then maternal uncles, then maternal aunts, then daughters of paternal uncles, then their children in this order, then the client of wala. (Tali') Know that according to Abu Hanifa, non-asaba relatives have guardianship of marriage, and this is istihsan (juristic preference). The qiyas (analogy) is that non-asaba have no guardianship, and that is the view of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) due to the saying of Ali: 'Marriage is for the asaba'; and because guardianship is established only for preserving the kinship from being attributed to a non-compatible match, and preservation is exclusively for the asaba. Abu Hanifa argues that this guardianship is a guardianship of consideration, and consideration is realized by delegating to one who is specifically related due to kinship that inspires compassion. Then the author arranged the owners of prescribed shares in the book according to the order of the asaba, and gave precedence to the mother because she is never blocked, then the sister for father and mother, as she is blocked in the presence of a son, and so on until he placed dhawi al-arham after all because there is difference among the Companions regarding their inheritance. And his statement: 'Then for the ruler' due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'The sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian.' It is said that what is meant by this ruling is the judge who is in charge of the orphans who have no guardians. Muhammad said: There is no guardianship for other than the asaba, and when the asaba are absent, the guardianship belongs to the imam or his deputy, who is the judge. The three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad) said that. And the executor (wasi) may not marry the orphans unless the testator delegates that to him. ('Ayni, extract) Then for the ruler. And the distant guardian may marry in the absence of the near guardian by the distance of qasr (shortening of prayer), and it is not invalidated by his return. (Tali' meaning the distant guardian is permissible). And the guardian of an insane woman is her son, not her father. And the insane: meaning the near guardian. Then: meaning when these are absent, guardianship goes to the ruler. (Tali') For the ruler: meaning the sultan and the judge authorized for marriage, and the deputy of the judge is like the judge if he delegates that to him. (Tali') And the distant guardian's marriage: meaning when the near guardian is absent with a disconnected absence, the distant guardian has the guardianship of marriage. Zufar said: He has no guardianship because the guardianship of the near is established and is not nullified by his absence. Our argument: this is a guardianship of consideration, and there is no consideration in delegating to one whose opinion is not beneficial. Then there is difference regarding the interpretation of 'disconnected absence': some say that the near should be in a town that a caravan reaches only once a year; some say it is the minimum travel distance, which is the author's choice and many later scholars, and fatwa is given accordingly; and some say that the compatible suitor is lost waiting, and this is closer to fiqh. Al-Shafi'i said: The judge marries her. This is based on the view that when the guardian is absent, guardianship transfers to the judge. But according to us, it transfers to the distant guardian by consensus. And his statement: 'It is not invalidated by his return' means what the distant guardian contracted in the absence of the near is not invalidated after the near's return, contrary to Zufar who said the distant guardian's contract is invalidated. Our argument: the Lawgiver has made the distant become the near, and ability upon the original after achieving the objective by the substitute is not beneficial, just as one who prays with tayammum and then finds water, his prayer is not invalidated. ('Ayni, extract) The distance of qasr: which is three days and their nights, and fatwa is given accordingly. (Tali') The son not the father: this according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Muhammad said: Her father is her guardian because he is more compassionate; hence his guardianship is general in both person and property, and the son has no guardianship over property, so the father is more entitled. Their argument (the two shaykhs): the son is given precedence by the asaba, and this guardianship is based on that by text, and increase in compassion is not considered, like the maternal uncle with some asaba. There is no difference between original insanity and accidental insanity due to the existence of incapacity. Zufar said: No one marries her in accidental insanity. From Abu Yusuf in one narration: both are guardians, so whichever marries is valid, and when both are present, the father is given precedence out of respect. If instead of the father there is a grandfather with the son, then the same difference applies. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 203/1]
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on marriage guardians and compatibility in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It is a commentary (sharh) that expands on the matn's rulings. The central ruling is that no marriage guardianship exists for a slave, a minor, an insane person, or a non-Muslim over a Muslim woman. The commentary explains the reasoning: these individuals lack full legal capacity over themselves, so they cannot exercise authority over others. For the slave, this negation is specifically for marriage, as he still has certain capacities like giving testimony or having authority over his free wife in marital matters. For the non-Muslim over a Muslim woman, the Quranic verse 4:141 is cited, establishing that non-Muslims have no authority over believers. However, a non-Muslim father retains guardianship over his minor daughter. The passage then details the order of guardians when there is no asaba (agnatic relative), according to Abu Hanifa's view. This order begins with the mother, then the sister for both parents, then for father only, then brothers and sisters for mother, then dhawi al-arham (uterine relatives), and finally the client of wala (mawla al-muwalah). A significant intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: Abu Hanifa's position is that non-asaba relatives can be guardians based on istihsan, because the underlying rationale is the welfare and compassion of close relatives. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold the qiyas (analogy) that guardianship belongs exclusively to the asaba, citing a hadith of Ali. The text discusses the difference between the two shaykhs and Muhammad regarding the guardian of an insane woman: according to the two shaykhs, her son is the guardian, but Muhammad says it is her father because of greater compassion. The passage also addresses the distant guardian marrying in the absence of the near guardian by a travel distance of qasr, and that such a marriage is not invalidated by the near guardian's return, contrary to Zufar's view. The commentary uses analogies from tayammum and water to illustrate that once a valid substitute act is performed, the original condition does not nullify it. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that the minimum travel distance for absence is three days and nights, and that the distant guardian's marriage is valid.

Key Terms
الولاية (al-wilayah): Legal authority to contract a marriage on behalf of another person, based on kinship or legal capacity.
العصبة (al-'asabah): Agnatic relatives who inherit by virtue of male lineage, such as sons, fathers, brothers, and paternal uncles.
ذوو الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): Uterine relatives who are not 'asaba, such as maternal uncles and aunts, daughters of paternal uncles, etc.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference: departing from a qiyas (analogy) due to a stronger evidence or consideration of public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), who is the marriage guardian for an adult Muslim woman who has no asaba relative?
A) The judge only
B) Her mother or other non-asaba relatives
C) A male non-Muslim relative
D) No one; she marries herself

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, non-asaba relatives can act as marriage guardians due to istihsan, and this is the relied-upon position.

Nikah — Marriage

Distant Guardian's Right to Marry in Absence of Close Guardian
Ruling with proof on the distant wali's authority during the close wali's absence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the distant guardian's authority during the close guardian's disconnected absence, apply the definition of disconnected absence, and explain the qiyas proof that the contract remains valid after the close guardian's return.

Core Idea
When the close guardian is absent at travel distance, the distant guardian may marry and the contract stands even after return.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on who may contract marriage when the closest guardian is unavailable. The key issue is how absence triggers the transfer of guardianship to a more distant relative. We will explore the definition of 'disconnected absence' and the proof by analogy that the marriage remains valid even after the close guardian returns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the distant guardian contracting marriage for the woman during the close guardian's absence. The Hanafi ruling is that this marriage is valid (sahih) and not invalidated by the close guardian's later return.
Contrast with Zufar's view that the contract is invalidated.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to the case of tayammum: just as prayer performed with tayammum remains valid even if water becomes available later, so too the marriage contracted by the substitute guardian remains valid when the original guardian returns.
The explicit analogy is stated: 'as if one prayed with tayammum then found water, his prayer is not invalidated'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative cause (illah) is that the substitute (khalaf) fulfills the purpose in the absence of the original. Once the purpose is achieved, the later ability to use the original does not undo the valid act.
The distant guardian becomes the effective guardian (ja'ala al-shar' al-ab'ada al-aqraba).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Zufar (within Hanafi school) argued that the close guardian's authority persists, so the distant guardian has no authority and his contract is invalidated upon return. Al-Shafi'i held that in absence, authority transfers to the ruler, not the distant relative.
The Hanafi consensus (ijma') holds that it transfers to the distant relative, not the ruler.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that the distant guardian's marriage is valid and not invalidated. This is supported by the clear qiyas and the practice of later Hanafi scholars like al-Marghinani.
The definition of disconnected absence is settled as the distance of qasr (three days and nights).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الأولياء والأكفاء. ثم للحاكم، وللأبعد التزويج بغيبة الأقرب مسافة القصر، ولا يبطل بعوده. (ط) في أي يجوز للولي الأبعد. ثم أي عند عدم هؤلاء تكون الولاية للحاكم. (ط) للحاكم: أي السلطان والقاضي المأذون بالنكاح ونائب القاضي كالقاضي إن فوض له ذلك. (ط) وللأبعد التزويج: أي إذا غاب الولي الأقرب غيبة منقطعة فللأبعد ولاية التزويج وقال زفر: لا ولاية له؛ لأن ولاية الأقرب قائمة فلا تبطل بغيبته ولنا أن هذه ولاية نظرية، ولا نظر في التفويض إلى من ينتفع برأيه، ثم في تفسير الغيبة المنقطعة اختلاف فقيل: أن يكون الأقرب في بلدة لا تصل إليها قافلة في السنة إلا مرة واحدة، وقيل: هي أدنى مدة السفر، وهو اختيار المصنف وكثير من المتأخرين وعليه الفتوى، وقيل: أن يفوت الكفؤ الخاطب بانتظاره، وهذا أقرب إلى الفقه وقال الشافعي يزوجها الحاكم، وهذا مبني على أنه إذا غاب الولي انتقلت الولاية إلى الحاكم، لكن عندنا تنتقل إلى الأبعد إجماعاً، وقوله: "ولا يبطل بعوده" أي لا يبطل ما عقد الأبعد في غيبة الأقرب بعد عود الأقرب خلافاً لزفر، حيث قال: يبطل عقد الأبعد، ولنا: أن الشرع جعل الأبعد الأقرب فالقدرة على الأصل بعد حصول المقصود بالخلف لا يفيد كما لو صلى بالتيمم ثم قدر على الماء لا يبطل ما صلى. (عيني، مستخلص) مسافة القصر : وهي ثلاثة أيام ولياليها وعليه الفتوى. (ط)
Chapter of Guardians and Compatible Matches. Then for the ruler, and for the distant relative to marry in the absence of the close relative by the distance of qasr, and it is not invalidated by his return. (T) i.e., it is permissible for the distant guardian. Then, i.e., in the absence of these, guardianship belongs to the ruler. (T) For the ruler: i.e., the sultan and the judge authorized for marriage and the deputy of the judge like the judge if he delegated that to him. (T) And for the distant relative to marry: i.e., if the close guardian is absent in a disconnected absence, then the distant relative has the guardianship of marriage. Zufar said: He has no guardianship because the guardianship of the close relative is still in effect and is not nullified by his absence. Our proof: This is a guardianship based on consideration, and there is no consideration in delegating to one who benefits from his opinion. Then regarding the interpretation of disconnected absence, there is difference: it is said that the close relative is in a town that a caravan only reaches once a year; and it is said that it is the minimum travel distance, which is the choice of the author and many later scholars, and upon it is the fatwa; and it is said that the compatible suitor would be lost by waiting, and this is closer to fiqh. Al-Shafi'i said: The ruler marries her. This is based on the principle that if the guardian is absent, guardianship transfers to the ruler, but according to us it transfers to the distant relative by consensus. His statement: 'and it is not invalidated by his return' means that what the distant relative contracted in the absence of the close relative is not invalidated after the close relative's return, contrary to Zufar, who said: The contract of the distant relative is invalidated. Our proof: The Lawgiver made the distant relative the close relative, so ability upon the original after achieving the purpose by the substitute does not avail, just as if one prayed with tayammum then found water, his prayer is not invalidated. (Ayni, Mukhtasar) Distance of qasr: It is three days and nights, and upon it is the fatwa. (T)
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the authority of a distant guardian (al-ab'ad) to contract marriage when the close guardian (al-aqrab) is absent. The text states that if the close guardian is absent for a 'disconnected absence'—defined here as the distance of qasr (three days and nights, which is the relied-upon opinion)—the distant guardian may validly marry the woman. Unlike Zufar, who argued that the close guardian's authority persists, the Hanafi position is that the distant guardian becomes the effective guardian in such cases, analogized to the case of tayammum: once prayer is validly performed with tayammum, the later availability of water does not invalidate it. Similarly, once the marriage is validly contracted, the close guardian's return does not annul it. The passage also notes al-Shafi'i's view that authority transfers to the ruler, but the Hanafi school maintains by consensus that it transfers to the distant relative. The definition of disconnected absence is clarified: the relied-upon position is the minimum travel distance (three days and nights), though other jurists considered loss of a suitable suitor as the criterion.

Key Terms
غيبة منقطعة (ghaybah munqati'ah): An absence of the close guardian such that he is at a distance of qasr (three days' travel) or where a suitable suitor would be lost by waiting.
ولاية نظرية (wilayah nazariyyah): Guardianship based on the guardian's ability to consider and act in the best interest; here, the distant guardian's authority is of this type.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the relied-upon definition of 'disconnected absence' (ghaybah munqati'ah) for the purpose of transferring guardianship to the distant relative?
A) The close guardian is in a country that a caravan visits only once a year.
B) The close guardian is at the distance of qasr, which is three days and nights.
C) The close guardian has not returned for one lunar year.
D) The suitable suitor would be lost by waiting any further.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that when the close guardian is absent at travel distance, the distant guardian may validly marry, and the contract remains valid even after the close guardian's return, by analogy to tayammum.
