Definition and Dispute on Kafa'ah in Marriage
Definition and inter-madhhab dispute on kafa'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on kafa'ah, identify the opposing view of Malik and Sufyan, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf in practical terms.

Core Idea
Kafa'ah in marriage protects family honour; Hanafi includes six matters, Malik and Sufyan only religion.

Compatibility (kafa'ah) between spouses is a key concept in Hanafi marriage law, especially when a woman marries herself without a guardian. This passage defines kafa'ah and presents a classical dispute: does compatibility extend beyond religion to lineage, profession, and wealth? Understanding this ikhtilaf illuminates how each school balances textual proofs with social custom.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Examine the definition: kafa'ah is equality in specific matters, considered in men only, across six things (elaborated elsewhere in the matn). Note its function: a condition for bindingness (luzum) on the guardian.
Guardian can annul the marriage if husband is not compatible.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Malik and Sufyan al-Thawri restrict kafa'ah exclusively to religion (din). Recognize their primary textual proofs: the hadith 'People are equal like the teeth of a comb' and Quran 49:13.
They argue that any other basis contradicts Islamic equality.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Analyze the opposing dalil: the hadith and ayah emphasize taqwa as the sole measure of superiority. Understand how this logically leads to restricting kafa'ah to religion only.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: those proofs pertain to the Hereafter, not to worldly marriage rulings. They cite the hadith of Jabir: 'Women are married only to compatible men,' which establishes compatibility as a worldly consideration.
Hanafi usul: amr in 'la yuzawwijun illa min al-akfa'' indicates obligation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Hanafi law, a guardian can annul a marriage if the husband is unequal in lineage, profession, etc. Under Maliki/Sufyan, only religious incompatibility gives the guardian that right.
This directly affects the bindingness of marriage contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكفاءة: لما كانت الكفاءة شرطاً في اللزوم على الولي إذا عقدت المرأة بنفسها كان اعتبارها فرع وجود الولي فقدم بيان الأولياء ثم أعقبه ببيان الكفاءة، وهي بالفتح والمد مصدر، والاسم منه الكفؤ، وهو النظير، والمراد هنا المساواة في أمور خاصة، أو كون المرأة أدنى؛ لاستنكاف أن تكون فراشا للدني بخلاف العكس، والكفاءة في النكاح معتبرة في الرجال لا النساء في ستة أشياء، كما بين في المتن على سبيل التفصيل وقال مالك :وسفيان لا تعتبر إلا في الدين؛ لقوله عليه السلام: "الناس سواسية كأسنان المشط، لا فضل لعربي على عجمي إلا بالتقوى"، وقال تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ أَكْرَمَكُمْ عِنْدَ اللَّهِ أَتْقَاكُمْ﴾ (الحجرات: ۱۳) ولنا ما روى جابر أنه عليه السلام قال: "لا يزوج النساء إلا الأولياء، ولا يزوجن إلا من الأكفاء"، وما روياه في أحكام الآخرة وكلامنا في الدنيا. (فتح)
Section on Compatibility: When compatibility is a condition for bindingness upon the guardian if the woman marries herself, then its consideration is a branch of the existence of the guardian, so the statement of guardians was given precedence, then followed by a statement of compatibility. It (kafa'ah) is a verbal noun with fatha and madd, and the noun from it is kuf', meaning equal. Intended here is equality in specific matters, or that the woman is lower; because it is improper for her to be a bed for a lowly man, contrary to the opposite. Compatibility in marriage is considered in men, not in women, in six things, as detailed in the text. Malik and Sufyan said: It is only considered in religion due to his saying (peace be upon him): "People are equal like the teeth of a comb; no superiority of an Arab over a non-Arab except by piety." And the Almighty said: "Indeed, the most noble of you in the sight of Allah is the most righteous of you" (49:13). Our proof: Jabir narrated that he (peace be upon him) said: "Women are married only by guardians, and they are married only to compatible men." And what they narrated pertains to the rulings of the Hereafter, while we are speaking about this world. (Fath)
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of kafa'ah (compatibility) in marriage and presents an inter-madhhab dispute. According to the Hanafi school, kafa'ah is required in men (not women) in six matters (elaborated elsewhere). It is a condition for the bindingness (luzum) of the marriage contracted by the woman herself, meaning the guardian has the right to object if the husband is not compatible. The passage contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Malik and Sufyan al-Thawri, who restrict compatibility to religion only, citing the hadith 'People are equal...' and Quran 49:13. The Hanafi response is that the quoted hadith applies to the Hereafter, not to worldly rulings of marriage. They rely on the hadith from Jabir that marriage should only be to compatible men, which establishes compatibility as a worldly consideration to protect the family's honour.

Key Terms
كفاءة (kafa'ah): Compatibility or equality between spouses in specific matters, primarily for the husband, including lineage, religion, profession, and wealth.
كفؤ (kuf'): An equal or suitable match; the noun derived from kafa'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, on what basis is kafa'ah (compatibility) required for the husband in marriage?
A) Quran 49:13 about piety
B) Hadith 'People are equal like the teeth of a comb'
C) Hadith 'Women are married only to compatible men'
D) Consensus of all scholars

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school includes six matters for kafa'ah, citing a hadith of the Prophet that marriage should be to compatible men, and rebuts the opposing view by distinguishing between worldly and hereafter rulings.

intermediate
7 min
The Wali's Right to Annul (Tafriq) for Incompatibility
Rulings on the wali's right to annul and nature of tafriq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which walis have the right to annul an incompatible marriage, the timing of that right, and the legal effect of the annulment.

Core Idea
The wali's annulment is a judicial separation, not a divorce, and the right exists only before childbirth.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses the rights of the wali when a woman marries someone incompatible. The text clarifies that only the 'asabah (agnatic guardians) have the right to annul, not other relatives. The annulment must be sought before a judge and is not a divorce; if no child has been born, the wali may exercise this right, but after childbirth the right is lost.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wali who can annul
The text states only the 'asabah, even if not a mahram (e.g., paternal cousin), have the right to annul. This is to repel harm from the wali himself, as people reproach each other for lack of compatibility.
The asabah are the agnatic male relatives.
2
Determine the timing of the right
The wali can annul only before the wife gives birth to a child from that husband. After childbirth, the right lapses to avoid harming the child.
If no child is born, the right remains indefinitely.
3
Understand the nature and procedure
The annulment is not a divorce; it is a judicial separation (tafriq) executed by the judge. Until the judge separates, the rules of divorce and inheritance still apply.
This means the marriage is valid until annulled, and the annulment does not count as a divorce.
4
Determine mahr consequences
If the marriage is annulled before consummation, no mahr is due. If consummation occurred, the specified mahr is owed.
This follows the general rule for voidable marriages.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam Malik and Sufyan al-Thawri do not recognize compatibility except in religion, so they deny the wali any right to annul based on other criteria.
The Hanafi school considers multiple factors of compatibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرق الولي: أي العصبة لا غيره، وإن لم يكن محرماً كابن العم على الصحيح دفعاً للضرر عن نفسه؛ لأن الناس يتعايرون بعدم الكفاءة، وفرق ما لم تلد المرأة من الزوج إن شاء؛ لأن التفريق ليس بواجب عليه، بل هو رخصة في حقه خلافاً لمالك وسفيان وأما إذا ولدت منه فلا حق له، ولا يكون التفريق بذلك إلا عند القاضي ما لم يفرق القاضي فحكم الطلاق والإرث قائم، وتلك الفرقة ليست بطلاق، ولا مهر لها إن لم يدخل بها، وإن دخل بها فلها المسمى، وإطلاق هذه المسألة دليل على رجوع محمد إلى قولهما في النكاح بغير ولي، وقال مالك وسفيان: ليس للولي التفريق؛ لعدم اعتبار الكفاءة عندهما إلا في الدين. (فتح، مسكين) البعض: أي لو تزوجت امرأة بغير كفؤ فرضي به بعض الأولياء سقط حق الباقين في النقض عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: لا يسقط دفعاً للضرر عنهم، فللولي الذي هو مثله أن لا يرضى؛ لأنه حق الكل فلا يسقط إلا برضا الكل، كالدين المشترك إذا أسقط أحد الدائنين حقه لا يسقط حق الآخر، ولهما أن الحق في الدين متعدد وحق الكفاءة واحد غير متجزئ؛ لثبوته بما لا يتجزأ فيثبت لكل على الكمال كولاية الأمان في الجهاد، وإذا أسقطه بعضهم لا يبقى حق الباقين. (فتح، مص) وقبض المهر: أي إذا قبض الولي المهر من الزوج غير الكفؤ أو قبض النفقة أو الكسوة لأجل المرأة يكون رضاً منه لإسقاط حق الاعتراض، وإذا سكت لا يكون رضاً؛ لأن في الوجه الأول وجد الرضاء دلالة، وفي الوجه الثاني لا؛ لأن السكوت عرف رضاً في حقوق يعد النطق عيباً فيها، وهذا ليس كذلك، وحق الاعتراض للأولياء إنما يكون ما لم تلد منه، فإذا ولدت لا يكون لهم حق الاعتراض، لئلا يضيع الولد (مستخلص) نحوه رضاء: بالرفع عطف على القبض كقبول الهدية من الزوج، ويجوز بالجر عطفا على المهر كقبض التجهيز. (ع)
The wali separates: i.e., the 'asabah, not others, even if not a mahram, like the paternal cousin, according to the sound view, to repel harm from himself because people reproach each other for lack of compatibility. He separates as long as the woman has not given birth to a child from the husband, if he wishes, because separation is not obligatory upon him; rather it is a concession on his part, contrary to Malik and Sufyan. But if she has given birth to a child from him, then he has no right. And separation is not done except by the judge; as long as the judge does not separate, the rulings of divorce and inheritance remain in effect, and that separation is not divorce. She is not entitled to any mahr if he has not consummated with her; if he has consummated, then she is entitled to the specified mahr. The absolute nature of this issue is evidence that Muhammad retracted to the view of the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) regarding marriage without a guardian. Malik and Sufyan said: The wali has no right to separate because they do not consider compatibility except in religion. (Fath, Miskin) 'al-Ba'd': i.e., if a woman marries someone who is not compatible and some of the guardians consent, the right of the remaining guardians to object falls away according to both parties (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf said: It does not fall away to repel harm from them, so the guardian who is like him may not be satisfied because the right belongs to all; it only falls away by the consent of all, like a joint debt if one creditor drops his right, it does not drop the right of the other. Their proof: The right in debt is multiple, while the right of compatibility is single and indivisible; since it is established by what is indivisible, it is established for each in full like the guardianship of trust in jihad. When some of them drop it, the right of the others does not remain. (Fath, Mus) 'Wa qabad al-mahr': i.e., if the wali accepts the mahr from the incompatible husband, or accepts maintenance or clothing on behalf of the woman, that constitutes consent from him to drop the right of objection. If he remains silent, it is not consent; because in the first case, consent is indicated, in the second it is not because silence is customarily considered consent in rights where speaking is considered a defect, but this is not such a case. The right of objection for guardians exists only as long as she has not given birth from him; if she gives birth, they have no right of objection, so that the child is not lost. (Mukhtasar) 'Nahwahu rida'': with dammah, it is coordinated with al-qabd like acceptance of a gift from the husband; it is also permissible with kasr, coordinated with al-mahr like acceptance of the trousseau. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage provides several rulings related to the wali's rights when the husband is not compatible. First, only the 'asabah (agnatic relatives) have the right to annul the marriage (tafriq), not other relatives. This right exists only before a child is born; after childbirth, the right is lost to avoid harming the child. The annulment itself is not a divorce and must be done by the judge; until then, the rulings of divorce and inheritance apply. The passage also notes that Muhammad seems to have retracted to the view of the two shaykhs on the issue of marriage without a guardian. Second, regarding consent: if one wali consents to the incompatible marriage, the right of other walis to object falls away according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf holds that each wali retains the right because the right is joint. The school's view is that the right is single and indivisible, so consent of one suffices. Third, acceptance of mahr by the wali constitutes implied consent, but silence does not. The right of objection lapses after childbirth.

Key Terms
فرق (farraqa): The wali's act of separating the spouses due to incompatibility; it is not a divorce but an annulment by judicial order.
رضا (rida): Consent of the wali, which can be explicit or implicit (e.g., accepting mahr), and drops the right to object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the wali lose the right to annul a marriage due to lack of compatibility?
A) Immediately upon the marriage contract
B) After consummation
C) After the wife gives birth to a child from the husband
D) After one year of marriage

Key Takeaway
The wali's right to annul is a judicial separation (tafriq), not a divorce, and is lost after childbirth.

advanced
8 min
Partial Consent of Walis: Effect on the Right to Object
Intra-Hanafi dispute on effect of partial consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on whether consent of one wali drops the right of others to object, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Consent of one wali suffices to drop the right of all walis to object, because the right is indivisible.

This passage also addresses the situation where multiple walis exist and only some consent to the incompatible marriage. The text presents the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) that the right of all walis falls away, and Abu Yusuf's opposing view that it does not. The underlying legal reasoning involves the nature of the right to object: is it joint and divisible or singular and indivisible?

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
When a woman marries an incompatible man and some but not all of her walis consent, the dispute concerns whether the remaining walis still have the right to object.
The walis here are the 'asabah guardians.
2
State the majority position
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two shaykhs), if one wali consents, the right of the remaining walis to object falls away entirely.
This is the mufta bihi view in the school.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf argues that the right does not fall away because the right is joint; each wali retains his own right to object. He analogizes to a joint debt: if one creditor drops his share, the others' shares remain.
Abu Yusuf's view is not the relied-upon position.
4
Understand the majority's proof
The majority counter that the right of compatibility is one indivisible right, like the guardianship of trust (wilayat al-aman) in jihad. Since it is indivisible, it is established for each wali in full, and when one drops it, none remains.
The analogy to debt is rejected because debt is divisible.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, if a woman's paternal uncle consents, her paternal cousin cannot later object. The consent of one wali is sufficient.
This applies only before childbirth; after childbirth, no right exists for any wali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الكل (al-kull): The entire group of walis; the right belongs to all collectively.
ولاية الأمان (wilayat al-aman): Guardianship of trust in jihad; an indivisible authority that is fully established for each individual.

D) The consenting wali must pay compensation.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the wali's right to object to incompatibility is indivisible; consent of one wali drops the right of all.

6 min
Implied Consent and Lapse of Right After Childbirth
Implied consent and lapse of objection right after childbirth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify acts that constitute implied consent from the wali and state when the right of objection lapses after childbirth.

Core Idea
Accepting mahr is implied consent; silence is not. The wali's right to object lapses after childbirth to protect the child.

The final part of the passage discusses how the wali can lose his right to object through implied consent, particularly by accepting the mahr. It also emphasizes that silence does not constitute consent. Additionally, the right of all walis lapses automatically after the wife gives birth to a child from the husband, to prevent harm to the child.

Guided Path
1
Identify acts that constitute implied consent
If the wali accepts the mahr (dowry), maintenance, or clothing from the husband on behalf of the wife, that is considered consent and drops his right to object.
These acts clearly indicate approval.
2
Understand that silence is not consent
The text explicitly states that if the wali remains silent, it is not consent. This is because the custom (urf) does not treat silence as consent in such matters.
In other contexts (e.g., sales), silence may be consent, but not here.
3
Note the timing of the right to object
The wali's right to object exists only before the wife gives birth to a child from the husband. After childbirth, the right lapses entirely.
This applies to all walis, not just the one who might have consented.
4
Reason for lapse after childbirth
The right lapses to avoid harming the child, who would suffer if the marriage is annulled after birth.
This protects the child's legitimacy and social standing.
5
Apply the rules
If a wali accepts the mahr before childbirth, he cannot later object. If he remains silent and does not accept anything, he may still object before childbirth. After childbirth, no objection is possible.
Implied consent must be explicit through action, not inaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(Fath, Mus) 'Wa qabad al-mahr': i.e., if the wali accepts the mahr from the incompatible husband, or accepts maintenance or clothing on behalf of the woman, that constitutes consent from him to drop the right of objection. If he remains silent, it is not consent; because in the first case, consent is indicated, in the second it is not because silence is customarily considered consent in rights where speaking is considered a defect, but this is not such a case. The right of objection for guardians exists only as long as she has not given birth from him; if she gives birth, they have no right of objection, so that the child is not lost. (Mukhtasar) 'Nahwahu rida'': with dammah, it is coordinated with al-qabd like acceptance of a gift from the husband; it is also permissible with kasr, coordinated with al-mahr like acceptance of the trousseau. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage provides several rulings related to the wali's rights when the husband is not compatible. First, only the 'asabah (agnatic relatives) have the right to annul the marriage (tafriq), not other relatives. This right exists only before a child is born; after childbirth, the right is lost to avoid harming the child. The annulment itself is not a divorce and must be done by the judge; until then, the rulings of divorce and inheritance apply. The passage also notes that Muhammad seems to have retracted to the view of the two shaykhs on the issue of marriage without a guardian. Second, regarding consent: if one wali consents to the incompatible marriage, the right of other walis to object falls away according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf holds that each wali retains the right because the right is joint. The school's view is that the right is single and indivisible, so consent of one suffices. Third, acceptance of mahr by the wali constitutes implied consent, but silence does not. The right of objection lapses after childbirth.

Key Terms
قبض المهر (qabd al-mahr): Acceptance of the dowry by the wali, which constitutes implied consent and drops his right to object.
سكوت (sukut): Silence; not considered consent in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the wali's silence regarding the marriage?
A) Silence is considered consent.
B) Silence is not consent.
C) Silence is only consent after one year.
D) Silence has no legal effect.

Key Takeaway
Implied consent from the wali requires a positive act like accepting mahr; silence does not constitute consent, and all rights lapse after childbirth.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Concept of Kafa'ah and the Six Criteria Across Schools
Defines kafa'ah and lists criteria with school comparisons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six Hanafi criteria for kafa'ah and evaluate why the Shafi'i school limits it to Islam alone.

Core Idea
Kafa'ah is a guardian's right, not a condition of validity; the Hanafi school uniquely recognizes six criteria while other schools restrict to religion.

The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq opens by clarifying that kafa'ah (suitability) in marriage is a matter of social harmony, not a pillar of validity. The Hanafi school defines six criteria that give the guardian a right to object if there is disparity. This lesson unpacks the concept, the legal nature of kafa'ah, and the major inter-madhhab dispute over which criteria matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal nature of kafa'ah
From the opening phrase 'la al-sukut' (not silence), note that kafa'ah is about the guardian's active right, not automatic validity. The marriage is valid but the guardian can seek annulment if suitability is lacking.
This distinguishes Hanafi view from those who treat kafa'ah as a condition of validity.
2
List the six Hanafi criteria
The passage enumerates: lineage (nassaban), freedom (hurriyya), Islam (islam), religiosity (diyana), wealth (mal), and profession (hirfa). Summarize them in order.
The verses from al-Hamawi confirm the six.
3
Compare Hanafi criteria with other schools
Read the section mentioning Malik (three opinions), al-Shafi'i and Ahmad (only Islam). Identify the differences.
Shafi'i relies on Quranic equality of believers; Hanafi uses hadith and social custom.
4
Analyze the Hanafi dalil: the hadith of suitability
The hadith 'Quraysh some are suitable for others...' is presented. Determine its thubut (zanni) and how it supports lineage-based criteria.
Hanafi uses this to establish categories of lineage; Shafi'i interprets it as guidance not obligation.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Hanafi, a Qurayshi woman's guardian can object to a non-Arab husband. Under Shafi'i, no objection based on lineage. Understand how this affects real contracts.
The khilaf is not about validity but about guardian's right to seek annulment.

(فتح) وحرفة: [ أي تعتبر الكفاءة أيضاً من حيث الحرفة وهي الصناع. (ع)] سميت بها؛ لأنه ينحرف إليها، وتسمى صنعة أيضاً، والظاهر أنها أعم من ،الصنعة فالبزاز والعطار كفؤان؛ لأن المعتبر في الحرفة التقارب لا حقيقة المساواة وعليه الفتوى والخفاف لا يكون كفؤا لهما وكذا الخياط والحداد ،والنجار وقال أبو يوسف: لا تعتبر الكفاءة في الحرف إلا أن تفحش كالحجام والحائك والدباغ، ووجه عدم اعتبارها أنه يمكنه التحول عنها، ولهما: أن الناس يتفاخرون بشرفها ويتعيرون بدناءتها وإن أمكن تركها يبقى عارها. (فتح) نقصت عن مهر مثلها: أي نقصاناً لا يتغابن الناس في مثله، أما لو كان يسيراً يكون عفواً، وقوله: "للولي" وهو العصبة لا غيره من الأقارب ولا القاضي لو كانت سفيهة، ولا فرق بين المحرم ،وغيره وهو المختار، أي يجوز للولي أن يفرق بينهما عند القاضي إن لم يلتزم إتمام مهر مثلها، وهذه الفرقة لا يكون طلاقاً؛ لأنها ليست من قبل الزوج، أو يتم مهرها إن التزم فإن قلت: لا فائدة في هذا الإتمام؛ لأنها تسقطه؟ قلت: فائدته إقامة حق الولي :
Section on Kafa'ah. Book of Nikah. Not silence. Kafa'ah is considered in lineage: Quraysh are suitable [for each other] and Arabs are suitable [for each other], and in freedom and Islam. 'Not silence' means the silence of the guardian after knowledge of the marriage is not consent, even if prolonged, as long as she has not given birth. (T) And kafa'ah is considered in lineage because people boast about lineages. (A) i.e., from the aspect of lineage. Al-Sayyid al-Hamawi has versified its consideration in two couplets: 'Indeed kafa'ah in marriage is in six, for which a beautiful house has recorded: lineage, Islam, likewise profession, freedom, religion, and wealth.' Only. And Malik said in one opinion: it is considered only in religion; and in another opinion: no kafa'ah at all; and in one opinion: it is considered in religion, freedom, and freedom from defects. According to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, it is considered only in Islam. And from Ahmad, also in lineage. And in one opinion among Shafi'is: it is considered in wealth and freedom from defects. (Fath) 'Quraysh are suitable [for each other]' means some Quraysh are suitable for others, and similarly some Arabs are suitable for others, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Quraysh, some of them are suitable for others, clan by clan; and Arabs, some of them are suitable for others, tribe by tribe; and the mawali, some of them are suitable for others, man by man.' Know that Quraysh are those from the descendants of al-Nadr; the Hashimi is from the descendants of Hashim ibn 'Abd Manaf; Arabs are those whose common ancestor is above al-Nadr; and the mawali are others. They are called mawali because they helped the Arabs, and the helper is mawla. Allah says: 'And the disbelievers have no mawla' (Muhammad: 11). Then his saying: 'clan by clan' means no distinction is considered between Quraysh from whatever clan they are. For example, 'Ali and 'Uthman: 'Uthman was Umawi, and 'Ali ibn Abi Talib married his daughter to 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, and 'Umar married his daughter to him, and he was 'Adawi, but all are Quraysh. So it is known that there is no regard for superiority of some clans of Quraysh over others. Similarly, Arabs, some of them are suitable for others, tribe by tribe, meaning no regard for superiority of some Arab tribes over others, except Banu Bahila, for they are known for baseness. Al-'Ayni said: If you say: Quraysh are also Arabs, so how can they be conjoined as separate, and Quraysh are mentioned specifically? I say: Because of the virtue of Quraysh, they are mentioned specifically, as if they are another species, except that other Arabs are not suitable for Quraysh. In al-Mabsut: The best of people in lineage are Banu Hashim, then Quraysh, then Arabs, due to the narration from him (peace be upon him): 'Indeed Allah chose from among people the Arabs, and from the Arabs Quraysh, and from them chose Banu Hashim, and chose me from Banu Hashim.' (Madkhal) And Arabs, all of them except Quraysh, are suitable for each other, but they are not suitable for Quraysh. (T, A). 'And freedom' is an accusative conjoined to his statement 'lineage,' meaning kafa'ah is also considered from the aspect of freedom. (A) 'And Islam' means from the aspect of Islam, and this is regarding non-Arabs, because they boast about these two rather than lineage. (A) 'And freedom and Islam' means kafa'ah is considered in freedom and Islam, so whoever was a slave or a freedman is not suitable for an originally free woman, and similarly whoever had a free father is not suitable for a woman whose both parents are free, because slavery is an effect of disbelief and contains humiliation, so it is considered in the ruling of kafa'ah. Similarly, whoever embraced Islam by himself is not suitable for a woman whose parents embraced Islam. (Madkhal) 'And both parents in these two are like the fathers' was said: 'And both parents in these two are like the fathers' meaning if a woman's both parents are free and Muslim, a man whose both parents are also free and Muslim is suitable, but a man with only one parent free is not. This is the apparent ruling of the riwaya and it is correct, because the origin of lineage in definition is the father and its completion is the grandfather, so more than that is not required. So whoever has one father in Islam and freedom is not suitable for one who has both parents in these two. From Abu Yusuf: he is suitable, i.e., in a place where grandfather's disbelief is not considered a defect. Evidence for this is their agreement that it is not a defect among the Arabs, because they do not rebuke for that. The basis in this is that boasting among mawali is about Islam and freedom, not lineage, because they lost their lineages and do not boast about them but rather about religion. Salman referred to this when the Companions boasted about lineages and the matter ended with him, so he said: 'My father is Islam.' (Madkhal and Fath) 'In these two' means in freedom and Islam, i.e., one who has both parents in these two is suitable like one who has more ancestors. (T) 'And religiosity' is also accusative, conjoined to 'lineage,' i.e., kafa'ah is also considered from the aspect of piety. (A) According to the two shaykhs [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf], because it is among the highest honors, such that if a woman, even daughters of righteous men, marries a sinful man, the guardians have the right of objection. Muhammad said: it is not considered unless it is outrageous, like a man who slaps people in the markets and is mocked, or who comes out drunk and children play with him, then he is not suitable for her. From Abu Yusuf: if he is openly sinful, he is not suitable; if he is hidden, he is suitable. This is close to Muhammad's statement. (Ayni, Fath) 'And wealth' means kafa'ah is also considered from the aspect of wealth. (A) That is, he must possess the dower and maintenance. As for the dower, because it is the compensation for her sexual union, so delivery is necessary. What is meant is the prompt dower, the amount they are accustomed to paying promptly. As for maintenance, because the firmness and continuity of marriage depend on it. What is meant is maintenance through earning, which is considered in the apparent riwaya, such that one unable for both or one of them is not suitable for her. From Abu Yusuf, he considered ability for maintenance but not dower. Kafa'ah in wealth is considered at both ends, such that if a wealthy woman marries someone who can afford dower and maintenance, the contract is invalidated. Abu Yusuf said: if he is able to pay what is prompt for her and earns what he spends on her for the day, then he is suitable. The correct view is that equality in wealth is not considered. (Fath) 'And profession' means kafa'ah is also considered from the aspect of profession, which is craft. (A) It is called that because one turns to it; also called trade. The apparent is that it is more general than trade, so the cloth merchant and perfume merchant are suitable, because what is considered in profession is closeness, not actual equality, and this is the basis for fatwa. The cobbler is not suitable for them, nor is the tailor, blacksmith, and carpenter. Abu Yusuf said: kafa'ah in profession is not considered unless it is demeaning, like the cupper, weaver, and tanner. The reason for not considering it is that one can change it. But the two [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad] argue that people boast about its honor and are demeaned by its lowliness, even if it is possible to leave it, its disgrace remains. (Fath) 'If it falls short of her mahr al-mithl' means a shortfall such that people do not overlook similar [in transactions]; if it is minor, it is excused. His statement: 'For the guardian' means the guardian is the 'asaba, no other relative, nor the judge if she is foolish. There is no difference between a mahram and non-mahram, and this is the chosen view. That is, it is permissible for the guardian to separate them before the judge if he does not commit to completing her mahr al-mithl. This separation is not divorce because it is not from the husband's side. Or he completes her mahr if he commits. If you say: there is no benefit in this completion because she can waive it? I say: its benefit is to uphold the right of the guardian.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with the conditions of kafa'ah (suitability) in marriage. It opens by stating that the silence of the guardian after knowledge of the marriage is not considered consent, contrary to some. Then it lists the criteria for kafa'ah recognized in the Hanafi school: lineage, freedom, Islam, religiosity (piety), wealth, and profession. The author notes that Imam Malik held various opinions: only in religion, or not at all, or in religion and freedom and freedom from defects. Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad consider only Islam, though Ahmad also adds lineage in one narration. The Hanafi position is the broadest, considering six factors. The text then provides detailed proofs: a hadith that Quraysh are suitable for each other, Arabs for each other, and mawali for each other, with explanation of categories. It clarifies that Quraysh include all clans without distinction, and Arabs are those above al-Nadr in lineage, while mawali are non-Arabs. The passage then discusses freedom and Islam: a slave or convert is not suitable for a freeborn Muslim woman with both parents free and Muslim. Similarly, one who has only one free parent is not suitable for one with two. Abu Yusuf differs, holding that it depends on local custom. For religiosity, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider piety as a criterion, while Muhammad restricts it to egregious sin. For wealth, the ability to pay prompt dower and provide maintenance is required, though Abu Yusuf only considers maintenance ability. The correct view (al-ashah) is that equality in wealth is not required beyond the ability to provide. For profession, the Hanafi view considers closeness of craft, not exact equality; Abu Yusuf limits it to demeaning professions. The passage concludes with the ruling on mahr: if the husband's mahr is less than mahr al-mithl by a significant amount, the guardian (asaba) can annul the marriage before the judge if the husband does not agree to complete it. This annulment is not a divorce. The benefit of completion is to uphold the guardian's right.

Key Terms
الكفاءة (al-kafā'ah): Equality or suitability in marriage between spouses in certain criteria such that the marriage is considered socially appropriate.
أكفاء (akfā'): Plural of kuf' meaning suitable or equal in status.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The dower comparable to that of women of similar status (father's side) in the same locality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many criteria are considered for kafa'ah in marriage?
A) Three: lineage, freedom, Islam
B) Four: lineage, freedom, Islam, profession
C) Six: lineage, freedom, Islam, religiosity, wealth, profession
D) Only Islam

Key Takeaway
Kafa'ah is a guardian's right based on six criteria in the Hanafi school, setting it apart from other schools that limit it to religion.

Nikah — Marriage
advanced
15 min
Detailed Breakdown of Kafa'ah Criteria and Intra-Hanafi Differences
Detailed exposition of each criterion and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding religiosity, wealth, and profession, and apply the guardian's right to annul for deficient mahr.

Core Idea
The six criteria have internal disputes among Hanafi imams; the mufta bihi position considers closeness in profession and ability for both dower and maintenance.

The previous lesson introduced the six criteria. This lesson delves into each criterion individually, highlighting where the three Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani) differ. It also covers the important rule regarding mahr shortfall, which gives the guardian a right to annul the marriage if the husband cannot provide mahr al-mithl.

Guided Path
1
Examine lineage hierarchy and freedom/Islam requirements
Read the sections on Quraysh, Arabs, and mawali. Note that freedom and Islam are considered, and a convert is not suitable for a born Muslim. Compare Abu Yusuf's view on grandparents.
Abu Yusuf allows suitability if the grandfather's disbelief is not considered a defect locally.
2
Analyze the religiosity (diyana) criterion
The passage states that according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, religiosity matters; Muhammad restricts it to egregious sin. Understand the reasoning.
Muhammad considers only open sinfulness that brings public shame.
3
Analyze the wealth (mal) criterion
The ability to pay prompt dower and provide maintenance is required. Abu Yusuf only considers maintenance ability. The correct view (al-ashah) is that equality in wealth is not required.
The father's ability to pay is the focus.
4
Analyze the profession (hirfa) criterion
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider all professions; Abu Yusuf limits to demeaning ones (like cupper, weaver, tanner). The fatwa is on closeness, not equality.
The text uses examples: cloth merchant and perfume merchant are suitable; cobbler not suitable for them.
5
Understand the mahr shortfall rule
If the husband's mahr is less than mahr al-mithl by a non-trivial amount, the guardian (asaba) can annul the marriage before a judge if the husband does not agree to complete it. This annulment is not divorce.
The annulment is a guardian's right, not a divorce; the marriage is dissolved by the judge.

(ط، ع) وحرية: بالنسب عطف على قوله: نسباً أي تعتبر الكفاءة أيضاً أ من حيث الحرية. (ع) وإسلاماً: أي من حيث الإسلام وهذا في حق العجم؛ لأنهم يفتخرون بهما دون النسب. (ع) وحرية وإسلاماً: أي الكفاءة تعتبر في الحرية والإسلام، فمن كان عبداً أو معتقاً لا يكون كفؤاً للحرة الأصلية، وكذا من كان أبوه حراً لا يكون كفؤاً لذات أبوين حرين؛ لأن الرق أثر الكفر، وفيه معنى الذل، فيعتبر في حكم الكفاءة، وكذا من أسلم بنفسه لا يكون كفؤاً لمن أسلم أبواه. فلا يشترط أكثر من ذلك، فمن له أب واحد في الإسلام والحرية لا يكون كفؤاً لمن له أبوان فيهما، وعن أبي يوسف: أنه يكون كفؤاً، أي في موضع لا يعد كفر الجد عيباً، ويدل على ذلك اتفاقهم على أنه ليس بعيب في العرب؛ لأنهم لا يعيرون بذلك، والأصل في هذا أن التفاخر فيما بين الموالي بالإسلام والحرية لا بالنسب؛ لأنهم ضيعوا أنسابهم ولا يفتخرون به بل بالدين وإلى ذلك أشار سلمان حين افتخرت الصحابة بالأنساب وانتهى الأمر إليه فقال أبي الإسلام. (مستخلص وفتح) فيهما: أي في الحرية والإسلام، أي من له أبوان فيهما يكون كفواً كمن له آباء. (ط) وديانة: [بالنصب أيضاً عطف على "نسبا"، أي تعتبر الكفاءة أيضاً من حيث التقوى. (ع)] عند الشيخين؛ لأنه من أعلى المفاخر، حتى أن امرأة . بنات الصالحين لو نكحت فاسقاً كان للأولياء حق الرد، وقال محمد: لا تعتبر إلا أن يفحش كرجل يصفع في الأسواق ويسخر منه أو يخرج سكران ويلعب به الصبيان فإنه لا يكون كفؤا لها، وعن أبي يوسف: إن كان معلنا بالفسق فغير كفء، وإن كان مستتراً فهو كفؤ وهو قريب من قول محمد. (عيني، فتح) ومالاً : [ أي تعتبر الكفاءة من حيث المال أيضاً. (ع)] وهو أن يكون مالكاً للمهر والنفقة، أما المهر؛ فلأنه عوض بضعها، فلا بد من تسليمه، والمراد المهر المعجل قدر ما تعارفوا تعجيله، وأما النفقة فلأن قوام الازدواج ودوامه بها، والمراد النفقة بطريق الكسب وهو المعتبر في ظاهر الرواية، حتى أن العاجز عنهما أو عن أحدهما لا يكون كفؤاً لها، وعن أبي يوسف أنه اعتبر القدرة على النفقة دون المهر، والكفاءة في الغنى تعتبر عند الطرفين حتى أن امرأة فائقة في يسارها لو تزوجت ممن يقدر على المهر والنفقة يرد عقدها، وقال أبو يوسف إن كان قادراً على إيفاء ما يعجل لها ويكتسب ما ينفقه عليها يوماً فإنه يكون كفؤاً، والأصح أن المساواة في الغنى لا يعتبر. (فتح) وحرفة: [ أي تعتبر الكفاءة أيضاً من حيث الحرفة وهي الصناع. (ع)] سميت بها؛ لأنه ينحرف إليها، وتسمى صنعة أيضاً، والظاهر أنها أعم من ،الصنعة فالبزاز والعطار كفؤان؛ لأن المعتبر في الحرفة التقارب لا حقيقة المساواة وعليه الفتوى والخفاف لا يكون كفؤا لهما وكذا الخياط والحداد ،والنجار وقال أبو يوسف: لا تعتبر الكفاءة في الحرف إلا أن تفحش كالحجام والحائك والدباغ، ووجه عدم اعتبارها أنه يمكنه التحول عنها، ولهما: أن الناس يتفاخرون بشرفها ويتعيرون بدناءتها وإن أمكن تركها يبقى عارها. (فتح) نقصت عن مهر مثلها: أي نقصاناً لا يتغابن الناس في مثله، أما لو كان يسيراً يكون عفواً، وقوله: "للولي" وهو العصبة لا غيره من الأقارب ولا القاضي لو كانت سفيهة، ولا فرق بين المحرم ،وغيره وهو المختار، أي يجوز للولي أن يفرق بينهما عند القاضي إن لم يلتزم إتمام مهر مثلها، وهذه الفرقة لا يكون طلاقاً؛ لأنها ليست من قبل الزوج، أو يتم مهرها إن التزم فإن قلت: لا فائدة في هذا الإتمام؛ لأنها تسقطه؟ قلت: فائدته إقامة حق الولي :
Section on Kafa'ah. Book of Nikah. Not silence. Kafa'ah is considered in lineage: Quraysh are suitable [for each other] and Arabs are suitable [for each other], and in freedom and Islam. 'Not silence' means the silence of the guardian after knowledge of the marriage is not consent, even if prolonged, as long as she has not given birth. (T) And kafa'ah is considered in lineage because people boast about lineages. (A) i.e., from the aspect of lineage. Al-Sayyid al-Hamawi has versified its consideration in two couplets: 'Indeed kafa'ah in marriage is in six, for which a beautiful house has recorded: lineage, Islam, likewise profession, freedom, religion, and wealth.' Only. And Malik said in one opinion: it is considered only in religion; and in another opinion: no kafa'ah at all; and in one opinion: it is considered in religion, freedom, and freedom from defects. According to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, it is considered only in Islam. And from Ahmad, also in lineage. And in one opinion among Shafi'is: it is considered in wealth and freedom from defects. (Fath) 'Quraysh are suitable [for each other]' means some Quraysh are suitable for others, and similarly some Arabs are suitable for others, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Quraysh, some of them are suitable for others, clan by clan; and Arabs, some of them are suitable for others, tribe by tribe; and the mawali, some of them are suitable for others, man by man.' Know that Quraysh are those from the descendants of al-Nadr; the Hashimi is from the descendants of Hashim ibn 'Abd Manaf; Arabs are those whose common ancestor is above al-Nadr; and the mawali are others. They are called mawali because they helped the Arabs, and the helper is mawla. Allah says: 'And the disbelievers have no mawla' (Muhammad: 11). Then his saying: 'clan by clan' means no distinction is considered between Quraysh from whatever clan they are. For example, 'Ali and 'Uthman: 'Uthman was Umawi, and 'Ali ibn Abi Talib married his daughter to 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, and 'Umar married his daughter to him, and he was 'Adawi, but all are Quraysh. So it is known that there is no regard for superiority of some clans of Quraysh over others. Similarly, Arabs, some of them are suitable for others, tribe by tribe, meaning no regard for superiority of some Arab tribes over others, except Banu Bahila, for they are known for baseness. Al-'Ayni said: If you say: Quraysh are also Arabs, so how can they be conjoined as separate, and Quraysh are mentioned specifically? I say: Because of the virtue of Quraysh, they are mentioned specifically, as if they are another species, except that other Arabs are not suitable for Quraysh. In al-Mabsut: The best of people in lineage are Banu Hashim, then Quraysh, then Arabs, due to the narration from him (peace be upon him): 'Indeed Allah chose from among people the Arabs, and from the Arabs Quraysh, and from them chose Banu Hashim, and chose me from Banu Hashim.' (Madkhal) And Arabs, all of them except Quraysh, are suitable for each other, but they are not suitable for Quraysh. (T, A). 'And freedom' is an accusative conjoined to his statement 'lineage,' meaning kafa'ah is also considered from the aspect of freedom. (A) 'And Islam' means from the aspect of Islam, and this is regarding non-Arabs, because they boast about these two rather than lineage. (A) 'And freedom and Islam' means kafa'ah is considered in freedom and Islam, so whoever was a slave or a freedman is not suitable for an originally free woman, and similarly whoever had a free father is not suitable for a woman whose both parents are free, because slavery is an effect of disbelief and contains humiliation, so it is considered in the ruling of kafa'ah. Similarly, whoever embraced Islam by himself is not suitable for a woman whose parents embraced Islam. (Madkhal) 'And both parents in these two are like the fathers' was said: 'And both parents in these two are like the fathers' meaning if a woman's both parents are free and Muslim, a man whose both parents are also free and Muslim is suitable, but a man with only one parent free is not. This is the apparent ruling of the riwaya and it is correct, because the origin of lineage in definition is the father and its completion is the grandfather, so more than that is not required. So whoever has one father in Islam and freedom is not suitable for one who has both parents in these two. From Abu Yusuf: he is suitable, i.e., in a place where grandfather's disbelief is not considered a defect. Evidence for this is their agreement that it is not a defect among the Arabs, because they do not rebuke for that. The basis in this is that boasting among mawali is about Islam and freedom, not lineage, because they lost their lineages and do not boast about them but rather about religion. Salman referred to this when the Companions boasted about lineages and the matter ended with him, so he said: 'My father is Islam.' (Madkhal and Fath) 'In these two' means in freedom and Islam, i.e., one who has both parents in these two is suitable like one who has more ancestors. (T) 'And religiosity' is also accusative, conjoined to 'lineage,' i.e., kafa'ah is also considered from the aspect of piety. (A) According to the two shaykhs [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf], because it is among the highest honors, such that if a woman, even daughters of righteous men, marries a sinful man, the guardians have the right of objection. Muhammad said: it is not considered unless it is outrageous, like a man who slaps people in the markets and is mocked, or who comes out drunk and children play with him, then he is not suitable for her. From Abu Yusuf: if he is openly sinful, he is not suitable; if he is hidden, he is suitable. This is close to Muhammad's statement. (Ayni, Fath) 'And wealth' means kafa'ah is also considered from the aspect of wealth. (A) That is, he must possess the dower and maintenance. As for the dower, because it is the compensation for her sexual union, so delivery is necessary. What is meant is the prompt dower, the amount they are accustomed to paying promptly. As for maintenance, because the firmness and continuity of marriage depend on it. What is meant is maintenance through earning, which is considered in the apparent riwaya, such that one unable for both or one of them is not suitable for her. From Abu Yusuf, he considered ability for maintenance but not dower. Kafa'ah in wealth is considered at both ends, such that if a wealthy woman marries someone who can afford dower and maintenance, the contract is invalidated. Abu Yusuf said: if he is able to pay what is prompt for her and earns what he spends on her for the day, then he is suitable. The correct view is that equality in wealth is not considered. (Fath) 'And profession' means kafa'ah is also considered from the aspect of profession, which is craft. (A) It is called that because one turns to it; also called trade. The apparent is that it is more general than trade, so the cloth merchant and perfume merchant are suitable, because what is considered in profession is closeness, not actual equality, and this is the basis for fatwa. The cobbler is not suitable for them, nor is the tailor, blacksmith, and carpenter. Abu Yusuf said: kafa'ah in profession is not considered unless it is demeaning, like the cupper, weaver, and tanner. The reason for not considering it is that one can change it. But the two [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad] argue that people boast about its honor and are demeaned by its lowliness, even if it is possible to leave it, its disgrace remains. (Fath) 'If it falls short of her mahr al-mithl' means a shortfall such that people do not overlook similar [in transactions]; if it is minor, it is excused. His statement: 'For the guardian' means the guardian is the 'asaba, no other relative, nor the judge if she is foolish. There is no difference between a mahram and non-mahram, and this is the chosen view. That is, it is permissible for the guardian to separate them before the judge if he does not commit to completing her mahr al-mithl. This separation is not divorce because it is not from the husband's side. Or he completes her mahr if he commits. If you say: there is no benefit in this completion because she can waive it? I say: its benefit is to uphold the right of the guardian.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with the conditions of kafa'ah (suitability) in marriage. It opens by stating that the silence of the guardian after knowledge of the marriage is not considered consent, contrary to some. Then it lists the criteria for kafa'ah recognized in the Hanafi school: lineage, freedom, Islam, religiosity (piety), wealth, and profession. The author notes that Imam Malik held various opinions: only in religion, or not at all, or in religion and freedom and freedom from defects. Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad consider only Islam, though Ahmad also adds lineage in one narration. The Hanafi position is the broadest, considering six factors. The text then provides detailed proofs: a hadith that Quraysh are suitable for each other, Arabs for each other, and mawali for each other, with explanation of categories. It clarifies that Quraysh include all clans without distinction, and Arabs are those above al-Nadr in lineage, while mawali are non-Arabs. The passage then discusses freedom and Islam: a slave or convert is not suitable for a freeborn Muslim woman with both parents free and Muslim. Similarly, one who has only one free parent is not suitable for one with two. Abu Yusuf differs, holding that it depends on local custom. For religiosity, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider piety as a criterion, while Muhammad restricts it to egregious sin. For wealth, the ability to pay prompt dower and provide maintenance is required, though Abu Yusuf only considers maintenance ability. The correct view (al-ashah) is that equality in wealth is not required beyond the ability to provide. For profession, the Hanafi view considers closeness of craft, not exact equality; Abu Yusuf limits it to demeaning professions. The passage concludes with the ruling on mahr: if the husband's mahr is less than mahr al-mithl by a significant amount, the guardian (asaba) can annul the marriage before the judge if the husband does not agree to complete it. This annulment is not a divorce. The benefit of completion is to uphold the guardian's right.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The dower comparable to that of women of similar status (father's side) in the same locality.
الحرفة (al-hirfa): Profession or craft, considered as a criterion for kafa'ah.
الديانة (al-diyāna): Religiosity, piety, considered for kafa'ah by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fatwa (mufta bihi), how is the profession criterion applied?
A) Exact equality of profession is required.
B) Only demeaning professions are considered.
C) Closeness of craft is considered, not equality.
D) Profession is not considered at all.

Key Takeaway
The detailed criteria of kafa'ah involve intra-Hanafi differences; the mufta bihi positions balance pragmatism with social norms.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
12 min
Kafa'ah: Lineage, Religion, Wealth, and Profession
Expansion of the four pillars of kafa'ah with intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four aspects of kafa'ah and explain the Hanafi position on each, including the intra-madhhab differences.

Core Idea
Kafa'ah requires suitability in lineage, religion, wealth, and profession; approximation suffices, and the guardian may object if any is lacking.

This lesson unpacks the four conditions of kafa'ah as detailed in Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary. You will learn the apparent (zahir) position of the Hanafi school and the differing views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on each aspect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four aspects of kafa'ah
From the passage, extract the four areas where suitability is considered: lineage (nasab), religion (diyanah), wealth (mal), and profession (hirafah).
2
Analyze lineage (nasab)
The apparent position requires two forefathers (father and grandfather) in Islam and freedom. Abu Yusuf holds that one suffices if not considered a defect in the locale. Understand the evidence from the story of Salman and the focus on Islam over lineage.
This is a clear intra-Hanafi dispute.
3
Analyze religion (diyanah)
According to the two teachers (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), piety is a factor; a righteous woman's guardian can object to a flagrant sinner. Muhammad considers only scandalous sin. Abu Yusuf also distinguishes open vs. hidden sin.
4
Analyze wealth (mal) and profession (hirafah)
Wealth: ability to provide prompt mahr and maintenance through earning. Abu Yusuf ignores mahr ability. Profession: approximation is sufficient, e.g., cloth merchant and perfumer are suitable. Abu Yusuf only considers vile professions like cupper. The fatwa is on approximation.
For wealth, the most correct view is that equality is not required.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi position and practical implications
Summarize that the mufta bihi is the apparent position requiring two forefathers, consideration of piety (with a high threshold), ability for both mahr and maintenance, and approximation in profession. Understand that the guardian's right to object is based on deficiency in any of these.

كتاب النكاح
فصل في الكفاءة
وأبوان فيهما كالآباء، وديانةً ومالاً وحرّفةً، ولو نقصت عن مهر مثلها فللولي أي لو نكحت امرأة كفوا ونقصت أي يجوز لوليها وأبوان فيهما كالآباء: هذا ظاهر الرواية وهو الصحيح؛ لأن أصل النسب في التعريف إلى الأب وتمامه الجد، فلا يشترط أكثر من ذلك، فمن له أب واحد في الإسلام والحرية لا يكون كفؤاً لمن له أبوان فيهما، وعن أبي يوسف: أنه يكون كفؤاً، أي في موضع لا يعد كفر الجد عيباً، ويدل على ذلك اتفاقهم على أنه ليس بعيب في العرب؛ لأنهم لا يعيرون بذلك، والأصل في هذا أن التفاخر فيما بين الموالي بالإسلام والحرية لا بالنسب؛ لأنهم ضيعوا أنسابهم ولا يفتخرون به بل بالدين وإلى ذلك أشار سلمان حين افتخرت الصحابة بالأنساب وانتهى الأمر إليه فقال أبي الإسلام. (مستخلص وفتح) فيهما: أي في الحرية والإسلام، أي من له أبوان فيهما يكون كفواً كمن له آباء. (ط) وديانة: [بالنصب أيضاً عطف على "نسبا"، أي تعتبر الكفاءة أيضاً من حيث التقوى. (ع)] عند الشيخين؛ لأنه من أعلى المفاخر، حتى أن امرأة . بنات الصالحين لو نكحت فاسقاً كان للأولياء حق الرد، وقال محمد: لا تعتبر إلا أن يفحش كرجل يصفع في الأسواق ويسخر منه أو يخرج سكران ويلعب به الصبيان فإنه لا يكون كفؤا لها، وعن أبي يوسف: إن كان معلنا بالفسق فغير كفء، وإن كان مستتراً فهو كفؤ وهو قريب من قول محمد. (عيني، فتح) ومالاً : [ أي تعتبر الكفاءة من حيث المال أيضاً. (ع)] وهو أن يكون مالكاً للمهر والنفقة، أما المهر؛ فلأنه عوض بضعها، فلا بد من تسليمه، والمراد المهر المعجل قدر ما تعارفوا تعجيله، وأما النفقة فلأن قوام الازدواج ودوامه بها، والمراد النفقة بطريق الكسب وهو المعتبر في ظاهر الرواية، حتى أن العاجز عنهما أو عن أحدهما لا يكون كفؤاً لها، وعن أبي يوسف أنه اعتبر القدرة على النفقة دون المهر، والكفاءة في الغنى تعتبر عند الطرفين حتى أن امرأة فائقة في يسارها لو تزوجت ممن يقدر على المهر والنفقة يرد عقدها، وقال أبو يوسف إن كان قادراً على إيفاء ما يعجل لها ويكتسب ما ينفقه عليها يوماً فإنه يكون كفؤاً، والأصح أن المساواة في الغنى لا يعتبر. (فتح) وحرفة: [ أي تعتبر الكفاءة أيضاً من حيث الحرفة وهي الصناع. (ع)] سميت بها؛ لأنه ينحرف إليها، وتسمى صنعة أيضاً، والظاهر أنها أعم من ،الصنعة فالبزاز والعطار كفؤان؛ لأن المعتبر في الحرفة التقارب لا حقيقة المساواة وعليه الفتوى والخفاف لا يكون كفؤا لهما وكذا الخياط والحداد ،والنجار وقال أبو يوسف: لا تعتبر الكفاءة في الحرف إلا أن تفحش كالحجام والحائك والدباغ، ووجه عدم اعتبارها أنه يمكنه التحول عنها، ولهما: أن الناس يتفاخرون بشرفها ويتعيرون بدناءتها وإن أمكن تركها يبقى عارها. (فتح) نقصت عن مهر مثلها: أي نقصاناً لا يتغابن الناس في مثله، أما لو كان يسيراً يكون عفواً، وقوله: "للولي" وهو العصبة لا غيره من الأقارب ولا القاضي لو كانت سفيهة، ولا فرق بين المحرم ،وغيره وهو المختار، أي يجوز للولي أن يفرق بينهما عند القاضي إن لم يلتزم إتمام مهر مثلها، وهذه الفرقة لا يكون طلاقاً؛ لأنها ليست من قبل الزوج، أو يتم مهرها إن التزم فإن قلت: لا فائدة في هذا الإتمام؛ لأنها تسقطه؟ قلت: فائدته إقامة حق الولي :
أن يفرق أو يُتمّ ،مهرها، ولو زوج طفله غير كفؤ أو بغين فاحش صح، ولم يجز
أي الزوج أي مهر مثلها أي ولد الصغير أي النكاح عليهما
ذلك لغير الأب والجد.
The Book of Marriage. Section on Kafa'ah. And two forefathers in them are like forefathers, and in religion, wealth, and profession. And if she is deficient in her mahr al-mithl, then the guardian has the right: if a woman marries a kuf' and is deficient, her guardian has the right. 'And two forefathers in them are like forefathers': this is the apparent (zahir al-riwayah) and it is correct because the origin of lineage in definition goes to the father and its completion to the grandfather; nothing more is required. So one who has one father in Islam and freedom is not a kuf' for one who has two forefathers in them. And from Abu Yusuf: that he is a kuf', i.e., in a place where the grandfather's lineage is not considered a defect. And evidence for that is their agreement that it is not a defect among the Arabs because they are not disgraced by that. And the basis of this is that boasting among the mawali is about Islam and freedom, not lineage, because they have lost their lineages and do not boast about them but about religion. Salman alluded to that when the Companions boasted about lineages and the matter ended with him, and he said: My father is Islam. (Mukhtasar and Fath) 'In them': i.e., in freedom and Islam; i.e., one who has two forefathers in them is a kuf' like one who has ancestors. (T) 'And in religion': (also in the accusative as an adjective to 'lineage', i.e., kafa'ah is also considered in terms of piety. (A)) According to the two teachers (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) because it is among the highest points of pride. So much so that if a woman from the righteous women marries a faasiq (sinner), the guardians have the right to object. And Muhammad said: It is not considered unless it is excessive, like a man who slaps in the markets and is mocked, or comes out drunk and children play with him; then he is not a kuf' for her. And from Abu Yusuf: if he is open in sin, he is not a kuf'; if he is hidden, he is a kuf'. And this is close to Muhammad's view. (Ayni, Fath) 'And in wealth': i.e., kafa'ah is also considered in terms of wealth. (A) Which is that he possess the mahr and maintenance. As for the mahr, because it is the compensation for her private part, so it must be delivered. What is meant is the prompt mahr to the extent that it is customary to pay promptly. As for maintenance, because the foundation and continuity of marriage depend on it. What is meant is maintenance through earning, and that is what is considered in the apparent (zahir al-riwayah). So much so that one who is incapable of both or one of them is not a kuf' for her. And from Abu Yusuf: he considered the ability for maintenance without the mahr. And kafa'ah in wealth is considered from both sides, so much so that if a woman extremely rich marries one who can afford the mahr and maintenance, the contract is rejected. And Abu Yusuf said: if he is able to pay what is expedited to her and earn what he spends on her daily, then he is a kuf'. And the most correct is that equality in wealth is not considered. (Fath) 'And in profession': i.e., kafa'ah is also considered in terms of profession, which is the craft. (A) It is called that because he turns (yanharif) to it. It is also called 'craft' and it appears that it is more general than craft. So a cloth merchant and a perfumer are kuf' because what is considered in profession is approximation, not actual equality, and upon that is the fatwa. And a slipper-maker is not a kuf' for them, likewise a tailor, a blacksmith, and a carpenter. And Abu Yusuf said: Kafa'ah in professions is not considered unless it is excessive, like a cupper, a weaver, and a tanner. The reason for not considering it is that it is possible to change from it. And to them (the two seniors): People boast about its nobility and are disgraced by its vileness, and even if it can be abandoned, its disgrace remains. (Fath) 'She falls short of her mahr al-mithl': i.e., a shortcoming that people do not overlook. As for if it is slight, it is disregarded. And his saying 'the guardian': he is the 'asaba (agnatic heir), not other relatives, nor the judge if she is foolish. And there is no difference between a mahram and other, and that is the chosen view. I.e., the guardian has the right to separate them before the judge if he does not commit to completing her mahr al-mithl. And this separation is not divorce because it is not from the husband's side. Or he completes her mahr if he commits. If you say: there is no benefit in this completion because she can drop it? I say: its benefit is to uphold the right of the guardian. That he separates or completes her mahr. And if he marries his minor child to a non-kuf' or with excessive disparity, it is valid, and it is not permissible for other than the father and grandfather.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rules of kafa'ah (suitability in marriage) from the Hanafi school, as found in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It covers four aspects: lineage (nasab), religion (diyanah), wealth (mal), and profession (hirafah).

First, regarding lineage: The base text states that having two forefathers (i.e., father and grandfather) in Islam and freedom is like having many ancestors. This is the established position of the school (zahir al-riwayah). The reason is that the core of lineage goes back to the father and its completion to the grandfather; anything more is not required. Thus, a man with only one father in Islam and freedom is not a suitable match for a woman with both father and grandfather in those qualities. Abu Yusuf differs, holding that he is suitable unless in a culture where having only one forefather is considered a defect. Evidence for Abu Yusuf's view is that Arabs do not consider it a defect, and among non-Arabs (mawali), boasting is based on Islam and freedom, not lineage. The commentator cites the story of Salman al-Farisi to support this.

Second, in religion (diyanah): According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, piety is a factor in kafa'ah, so a righteous woman's guardian can object if she marries a flagrant sinner. Muhammad says it is considered only if the sin is scandalous (e.g., being publicly drunk or mocked). Abu Yusuf also distinguishes between open and hidden sin; an open sinner is not a kuf'.

Third, in wealth (mal): The husband must possess the (prompt) mahr and the ability to provide maintenance (nafaqah) through earnings. This is the apparent ruling. If he lacks either, he is not suitable. Abu Yusuf considers only the ability for maintenance, not the mahr. However, the most correct view is that equality in wealth is not required; the focus is on the ability to fulfill financial obligations.

Fourth, in profession (hirafah): The commentator explains that approximation, not exact equality, is required. For example, a cloth merchant and a perfumer are suitable for each other. However, a slipper-maker is not suitable for them, and similarly a tailor, blacksmith, or carpenter. Abu Yusuf again takes a narrower view: only extremely low professions (cupper, weaver, tanner) are considered disqualifying, because one can change professions. But the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (al-Shaybani) is that people boast about noble professions and feel shame from vile ones, even if changeable.

The second part deals with the situation where a woman's mahr is less than her mahr al-mithl (the customary mahr for women of her status). The guardian (the 'asaba) has the right to seek separation (before a judge) if the husband does not complete the mahr. This separation is not a divorce. Alternatively, the guardian can ask the husband to complete the mahr. The benefit of requiring completion is to uphold the guardian's right, even if the wife could waive the excess.

Finally, the passage addresses the father's and grandfather's authority to marry their minor children to a non-kuf' or with excessive disparity in mahr (e.g., marrying a daughter for 10 dirhams when her mahr al-mithl is 1000). According to Abu Hanifa, this marriage is valid because the father (and grandfather) has complete judgment and chooses what is beneficial for his child. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that it is not valid because it diminishes the child's rights. The compromise is that if the disparity is slight, it is valid by consensus. However, for guardians other than the father and grandfather, such a marriage is not valid at all, to prevent harm to the minor.

Key Terms
الكفاءة (al-kafā'ah): Suitability of husband and wife in lineage, religion, wealth, profession, such that the guardian may object if lacking.
المهر (al-mahr): Bridal gift (dower) paid by the husband; prompt and deferred portions exist.
الولي (al-walī): Guardian of the bride, specifically the 'asaba (agnatic heir), who can object to non-kuf' marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent (zahir al-riwayah) position in the Hanafi school, how many forefathers in Islam and freedom are required for kafa'ah in lineage?
A) Only one (the father)
B) Two (father and grandfather)
C) Three (father, grandfather, and great-grandfather)
D) None – lineage is not considered

Key Takeaway
Kafa'ah requires suitability in lineage (two forefathers), piety, ability to provide mahr and maintenance, and profession with approximation; the guardian may object if any standard is unmet.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Guardian's Right: Deficient Mahr and Authority Over Minors
Explanation of the guardian's right regarding deficient mahr and the special authority of father/grandfather

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule regarding the guardian's right to seek separation or completion of mahr, and explain why the father and grandfather can marry minors to non-kuf' while other guardians cannot.

Core Idea
If mahr is deficient, the guardian may demand completion or annulment; only fathers and grandfathers may marry minors to non-kuf'.

This lesson covers the second part of the passage: the guardian's right when the husband pays less than the customary mahr (mahr al-mithl), and the special authority of the father and grandfather over minor children. You will learn the procedure for objection and the rationale behind the broader powers of ascendant guardians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation of deficient mahr
The passage states that if a woman's mahr is less than her mahr al-mithl by an amount that is not negligible (not overlooked), the guardian has the right to act.
Slight deficiencies are ignored (عفوا).
2
Understand the guardian's options
The guardian (specifically the 'asaba, not other relatives or the judge if she is foolish) can either demand the husband complete the mahr to the mahr al-mithl, or seek separation (annulment) before a judge.
This separation is not a divorce (talaq) because it originates from the guardian, not the husband.
3
Analyze the benefit of completing the mahr
The commentator addresses a possible objection: if the wife can later forgive the extra amount, what benefit is there? The answer: the benefit is to uphold the guardian's right.
4
Examine the father's and grandfather's authority over minors
If the father (or grandfather) marries his minor child to a non-kuf' or with a gross disparity in mahr (e.g., 10 dirhams for a woman whose mahr al-mithl is 1000), the marriage is valid according to Abu Hanifa. This is because they have full discretion (wilayah) over the child's interests.
Other guardians (like uncles) do not have this authority; such a marriage would not be valid for them.
5
Distinguish between slight and gross disparity
If the deficiency is slight, it is valid by consensus even for other guardians. The disagreement is only over gross disparity.

وأبوان فيهما كالآباء، وديانةً ومالاً وحرّفةً، ولو نقصت عن مهر مثلها فللولي أي لو نكحت امرأة كفوا ونقصت أي يجوز لوليها وأبوان فيهما كالآباء: هذا ظاهر الرواية وهو الصحيح؛ لأن أصل النسب في التعريف إلى الأب وتمامه الجد، فلا يشترط أكثر من ذلك، فمن له أب واحد في الإسلام والحرية لا يكون كفؤاً لمن له أبوان فيهما، وعن أبي يوسف: أنه يكون كفؤاً، أي في موضع لا يعد كفر الجد عيباً، ويدل على ذلك اتفاقهم على أنه ليس بعيب في العرب؛ لأنهم لا يعيرون بذلك، والأصل في هذا أن التفاخر فيما بين الموالي بالإسلام والحرية لا بالنسب؛ لأنهم ضيعوا أنسابهم ولا يفتخرون به بل بالدين وإلى ذلك أشار سلمان حين افتخرت الصحابة بالأنساب وانتهى الأمر إليه فقال أبي الإسلام. The Book of Marriage. Section on Kafa'ah. And two forefathers in them are like forefathers, and in religion, wealth, and profession. And if she is deficient in her mahr al-mithl, then the guardian has the right: if a woman marries a kuf' and is deficient, her guardian has the right. 'And two forefathers in them are like forefathers': this is the apparent (zahir al-riwayah) and it is correct because the origin of lineage in definition goes to the father and its completion to the grandfather; nothing more is required. So one who has one father in Islam and freedom is not a kuf' for one who has two forefathers in them. And from Abu Yusuf: that he is a kuf', i.e., in a place where the grandfather's lineage is not considered a defect. And evidence for that is their agreement that it is not a defect among the Arabs because they are not disgraced by that. And the basis of this is that boasting among the mawali is about Islam and freedom, not lineage, because they have lost their lineages and do not boast about them but about religion. Salman alluded to that when the Companions boasted about lineages and the matter ended with him, and he said: My father is Islam. (Mukhtasar and Fath) 'In them': i.e., in freedom and Islam; i.e., one who has two forefathers in them is a kuf' like one who has ancestors. (T) 'And in religion': (also in the accusative as an adjective to 'lineage', i.e., kafa'ah is also considered in terms of piety. (A)) According to the two teachers (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) because it is among the highest points of pride. So much so that if a woman from the righteous women marries a faasiq (sinner), the guardians have the right to object. And Muhammad said: It is not considered unless it is excessive, like a man who slaps in the markets and is mocked, or comes out drunk and children play with him; then he is not a kuf' for her. And from Abu Yusuf: if he is open in sin, he is not a kuf'; if he is hidden, he is a kuf'. And this is close to Muhammad's view. (Ayni, Fath) 'And in wealth': i.e., kafa'ah is also considered in terms of wealth. (A) Which is that he possess the mahr and maintenance. As for the mahr, because it is the compensation for her private part, so it must be delivered. What is meant is the prompt mahr to the extent that it is customary to pay promptly. As for maintenance, because the foundation and continuity of marriage depend on it. What is meant is maintenance through earning, and that is what is considered in the apparent (zahir al-riwayah). So much so that one who is incapable of both or one of them is not a kuf' for her. And from Abu Yusuf: he considered the ability for maintenance without the mahr. And kafa'ah in wealth is considered from both sides, so much so that if a woman extremely rich marries one who can afford the mahr and maintenance, the contract is rejected. And Abu Yusuf said: if he is able to pay what is expedited to her and earn what he spends on her daily, then he is a kuf'. And the most correct is that equality in wealth is not considered. (Fath) 'And in profession': i.e., kafa'ah is also considered in terms of profession, which is the craft. (A) It is called that because he turns (yanharif) to it. It is also called 'craft' and it appears that it is more general than craft. So a cloth merchant and a perfumer are kuf' because what is considered in profession is approximation, not actual equality, and upon that is the fatwa. And a slipper-maker is not a kuf' for them, likewise a tailor, a blacksmith, and a carpenter. And Abu Yusuf said: Kafa'ah in professions is not considered unless it is excessive, like a cupper, a weaver, and a tanner. The reason for not considering it is that it is possible to change from it. And to them (the two seniors): People boast about its nobility and are disgraced by its vileness, and even if it can be abandoned, its disgrace remains. (Fath) 'She falls short of her mahr al-mithl': i.e., a shortcoming that people do not overlook. As for if it is slight, it is disregarded. And his saying 'the guardian': he is the 'asaba (agnatic heir), not other relatives, nor the judge if she is foolish. And there is no difference between a mahram and other, and that is the chosen view. I.e., the guardian has the right to separate them before the judge if he does not commit to completing her mahr al-mithl. And this separation is not divorce because it is not from the husband's side. Or he completes her mahr if he commits. If you say: there is no benefit in this completion because she can drop it? I say: its benefit is to uphold the right of the guardian. That he separates or completes her mahr. And if he marries his minor child to a non-kuf' or with excessive disparity, it is valid, and it is not permissible for other than the father and grandfather.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rules of kafa'ah (suitability in marriage) from the Hanafi school, as found in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It covers four aspects: lineage (nasab), religion (diyanah), wealth (mal), and profession (hirafah).

First, regarding lineage: The base text states that having two forefathers (i.e., father and grandfather) in Islam and freedom is like having many ancestors. This is the established position of the school (zahir al-riwayah). The reason is that the core of lineage goes back to the father and its completion to the grandfather; anything more is not required. Thus, a man with only one father in Islam and freedom is not a suitable match for a woman with both father and grandfather in those qualities. Abu Yusuf differs, holding that he is suitable unless in a culture where having only one forefather is considered a defect. Evidence for Abu Yusuf's view is that Arabs do not consider it a defect, and among non-Arabs (mawali), boasting is based on Islam and freedom, not lineage. The commentator cites the story of Salman al-Farisi to support this.

Second, in religion (diyanah): According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, piety is a factor in kafa'ah, so a righteous woman's guardian can object if she marries a flagrant sinner. Muhammad says it is considered only if the sin is scandalous (e.g., being publicly drunk or mocked). Abu Yusuf also distinguishes between open and hidden sin; an open sinner is not a kuf'.

Third, in wealth (mal): The husband must possess the (prompt) mahr and the ability to provide maintenance (nafaqah) through earnings. This is the apparent ruling. If he lacks either, he is not suitable. Abu Yusuf considers only the ability for maintenance, not the mahr. However, the most correct view is that equality in wealth is not required; the focus is on the ability to fulfill financial obligations.

Fourth, in profession (hirafah): The commentator explains that approximation, not exact equality, is required. For example, a cloth merchant and a perfumer are suitable for each other. However, a slipper-maker is not suitable for them, and similarly a tailor, blacksmith, or carpenter. Abu Yusuf again takes a narrower view: only extremely low professions (cupper, weaver, tanner) are considered disqualifying, because one can change professions. But the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (al-Shaybani) is that people boast about noble professions and feel shame from vile ones, even if changeable.

The second part deals with the situation where a woman's mahr is less than her mahr al-mithl (the customary mahr for women of her status). The guardian (the 'asaba) has the right to seek separation (before a judge) if the husband does not complete the mahr. This separation is not a divorce. Alternatively, the guardian can ask the husband to complete the mahr. The benefit of requiring completion is to uphold the guardian's right, even if the wife could waive the excess.

Finally, the passage addresses the father's and grandfather's authority to marry their minor children to a non-kuf' or with excessive disparity in mahr (e.g., marrying a daughter for 10 dirhams when her mahr al-mithl is 1000). According to Abu Hanifa, this marriage is valid because the father (and grandfather) has complete judgment and chooses what is beneficial for his child. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that it is not valid because it diminishes the child's rights. The compromise is that if the disparity is slight, it is valid by consensus. However, for guardians other than the father and grandfather, such a marriage is not valid at all, to prevent harm to the minor.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The customary dower appropriate for a woman of her status (family, beauty, education, etc.).
الفرقة (al-furqah): Separation (annulment) of the marriage by the guardian's request, distinct from divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a father marries his minor daughter to a man who is not a kuf' and with a grossly deficient mahr, what is the ruling?
A) The marriage is void (batil) because it harms the child.
B) The marriage is valid, but only if the deficiency is slight.
C) The marriage is valid according to Abu Hanifa, and the father's authority stands.
D) The marriage is suspended until the child reaches puberty and can consent.

Key Takeaway
A guardian can demand completion of mahr or annulment if the mahr is significantly deficient; only fathers and grandfathers can marry minors to non-kuf'.
